neering, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfillment of the require­
ments for the degree of Cand.polyt. in the M.Sc. Civil Engineering program at Technical

to June 18, 2021 and is based on experiments made on DTU, with Professor Ole Mejl­
hede Jensen as supervisor. The defence will take place on June 25, 2021 at Technical
University of Denmark, Department of DTU Civil Engineering.

technology, and hopefully, the reader will benefit from reading this thesis. Furthermore, it
is hoped, that the thesis can be used for further research.

ii
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:5==
Abstract
The autogenous deformation phenomenon has become of particular importance when
silica fume and superplasticizer are developed as additives for production of high strength
concrete (HSC) or high performance concrete (HPC). Silica fume decreases the pore
size and superplasticizer makes it possible to reduce the w/c­ratio. The disadvantage is,
that self­desiccation shrinkage increases significantly, which composes a major part of
autogenous deformation. As a result, cracks in concrete may propagate, which constitute
a severe problem within strength, durability and aesthetics.
In this thesis the potential of using CaO as expansive additive for compensating the auto­
genous shrinkage is investigated. Due to the rapid hydration of free CaO, skin carbonation
is proposed to retard the hydration reaction, with the intention of creating an induction pe­
riod for the characteristic expansive reaction from hydration. This is expected to cause
an efficient shrinkage compensation, since skin carbonation may prevent the CaO from
hydrating before setting.
The autogenous deformation is investigated by measurements of the linear strain of ce­
ment pastes containing varying amounts of CaO and differ in degree of carbonation. The
measurements are conducted by the acknowledged dilatometer method at 30 °C and a
self­designed tube method at 20 °C, which has been conceived due to the large expan­
sions observed of the cement pastes in the dilatometer. Further investigations include
analyses of the hydration resistance, and the particles regarding size and fineness.
The results show, that untreated CaO is highly reactive and will compensate for the au­
togenous shrinkage, but large amounts are needed before expansion is reached. Skin
carbonating the expansive additive shows promising results, and the hydration resistance
increases significantly, retarding the expansive reaction. The autogenous deformation of
cement pastes is extremely affected when pre­treating the added CaO by skin carbon­
ation, observing expansions of cement pastes up to 8.6% after 4 days compared to a
reduction of 0.04% for a similar amount of CaO added, but without skin carbonation treat­
ment.
This suggests, that preliminary treatment of CaO by skin carbonation is efficient for utiliz­
ing the potential of CaO as expansive additive, with the intention to reduce or completely
compensate for the autogenous shrinkage.

1
Introduction
The use of concrete goes back to ancient Rome, but it is first within the last century,
that research within concrete technology has proliferated and made concrete the most
used building material in the world [Andersen, 2014]. A significant compound of concrete
is the composition of cement paste, which depends on the purpose of use. For high
strength concrete (HSC) or high performance concrete (HPC), widely known and used
in larger constructions today, superplasticizer and silica fume constitute a large part of
the additives in the cement paste. Superplasticizer is used to make concrete with low
w/c­ratio workable and the silica fume working as supplementary cementitious material
increases the strength significantly [Neville and Aïtcin, 1998].
Although these properties are substantial for an efficient concrete with higher strengths, it
is at the expense of considerable shrinkage from autogenous deformation. Larger auto­
genous shrinkage is a result of increase in internal tensile stresses from self­desiccation
shrinkage. These stresses may exceed the tensile strength of the cement body, and will
lead to propagation of cracks if restrained, which is a severe problem regards strength,
durability and aesthetics [Jensen and Hansen, 2001; Polat et al., 2015].
The autogenous shrinkage can be mitigated by adding expansive additives such as cal­
cium oxide (CaO) or magnesia (MgO) [Polat et al., 2015]. Such additives are reported
to hydrate rapidly, that may be a concern in regards to efficient shrinkage compensation,
since their hydration reaction may happen before setting [Collepardi et al., 2005]. Increas­
ing the hydration resistance of the expansive additives by skin carbonation is suggested
for retarding the hydration reaction for a more efficient shrinkage compensation, which
forms the foundation of the problem to be addressed in this thesis.
Scope
The purpose for this thesis is to investigate the effect of skin carbonation on hydration
of CaO. This cover the ability of CaO to compensate for autogenous shrinkage, and the
effect of skin carbonation as a retarding agent for the hydration reaction with the intention
of utilizing the potential of CaO for shrinkage compensation.
The primary investigations include measurements of autogenous deformation for cement
pastes with CaO as expansive additive and how skin carbonation affect the expansive
potential. The cement pastes varies in amount of CaO added and its degree of carbona­
tion (DOC). To support these examinations, the hydration resistance is analyzed for both
untreated­ and skin carbonated CaO.
For larger productions of skin carbonated CaO, preliminary investigations are required
for understanding the carbonation reaction circumstances, including thermogravimetric
analysis (TGA).
Furthermore, general experiments of CaO are made including examinations of the parti­
cles regarding size, specific surface area (Blaine fineness) and loss on ignition (LOI). The
influence of particle size is included in the experiments made.
The intention for this thesis has initially been to examine MgO as expansive additive, but
has later been changed to CaO due to issues with carbonation of MgO. The investigations
of carbonation by TGA and autogenous deformation are thereby also performed with MgO.
These experiments and results are described separately in Chapter 5.

==PAGE:15==
2
Background
2.1
Introduction to autogenous deformation
Henry Louis LeChâtelier, a French scientist and one of the pioneers within cement re­
search, was perhaps the first person to mention the phenomena chemical shrinkage and
self­desiccation happening from hydration of cement [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014]p.447−448.
Furthermore, he distinguished between an absolute volume and apparent volume of a
hardening cement paste. Further research has shown, that change in absolute volume
refers to chemical shrinkage, and a change in apparent volume refers to autogenous de­
formation as a result of self­desiccation [Jensen and Hansen, 2001].
His research took place in the early 1900s leading to several examinations of these phe­
nomena through time. However, research showed that the magnitude of autogenous
deformation for concrete was of minor importance compared to other phenomena such
as thermal deformation or drying shrinkage, why this phenomenon did not get any fur­
ther attention until the development of superplasticizers and silica fume in the early 1980s
[Jensen and Hansen, 2001]. These additives led to advent of HSC/HPC in concrete prac­
tice, but even though this opened a whole new world in concrete technology, the already
known phenomenon, i.e., autogenous deformation, suddenly became important. A num­
ber of concrete cracking cases were reported to a much larger degree than what had
been observed before. Investigations showed, that this was due to a significant increase
in self­desiccation and self­desiccation shrinkage as a result of a lower w/c­ratio and silica
fume addition [Tazawa, 1998]p.13−14.
The silica fumes addition changes the microstructure, decreasing the pore size of the hy­
dration products. In addition, hydration of silica fume has a high chemical shrinkage. The
increase in self­desiccation and self­desiccation shrinkage lead to a larger shrinkage of
the apparent volume; autogenous shrinkage [Jensen and Hansen, 2001].
2.2
Shrinkage compensation
Compensating for shrinkage is a subject where research goes back to the 1920s. Ex­
pansive cements were developed for commercial use and were defined as shrinkage­
compensating and self­prestressing by the American Concrete Institute. The first devel­
oped was by V. V. Mikhailov in 1955 called Type M expansive cement, while Alexander
Klein later developed the Type K expansive cement in 1958 containing sulfo­aluminate
(C4A3¯S), that has been more widely used [Fu et al., 1994]. A journal by The Cement As­
sociation of Japan from 1968 contain several papers that discuss usage and optimization
of expansive cements including Budnikov and Kravchenko [1968] as well as Klein and
Mehta [1968]. These expansive cements vary in composition and may include different
additives, among some of them are free calcium oxide (CaO) and free magnesia (MgO).
However, reliable results when using free CaO and ­MgO as expansive additive could not
be obtained at that point [Budnikov and Kravchenko, 1968].
As research continued and measuring equipment improved, many studies have proved
the effect of free CaO and ­MgO as expansive additives at compensating autogenous
shrinkage. According to Mo et al. [2015a], addition of free MgO shows to compensate
the autogenous shrinkage, and that it is governed by the amount of free MgO added [Mo
et al., 2012]. This is also applied to the use of free CaO according to Seo et al. [2020],
that also describe how the expansive additive enhances the early strength development
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
5
==PAGE:16==
of the cement paste, implying a possible reduction in setting time. Studies also show, that
the effect of expansive additives is dependent on other cementitious materials present in
the cement paste, including the amount of silica fume and fly ash [Mo et al., 2015b].
Concerns about the reaction rate of expansive additives are discussed by both Polat et al.
[2015] and Collepardi et al. [2005]. They illustrate several possibilities of using free CaO
and ­MgO as shrinkage reducing additive, yet this will only occur usefully, if the cement
paste has begun its stress development by forming a rigid solid, making it able to re­
strain the expansion. In other words, if the expansive reaction occurs too early, it may
not produce any advantageous compressive stresses, meaning that the compensation is
insignificant [Collepardi et al., 2005]. This especially applies to free CaO hydration com­
pared to hydration of free MgO, since the reaction of CaO happens more rapidly than of
MgO [Polat et al., 2015]. Nonetheless, retarding the expansive reaction from hydration of
free CaO or ­MgO may therefore be a solution to this issue.
Studies show, that the hydration reaction of free CaO can be retarded by skin carbonating
the oxide by CO2. This will create a thin layer of carbonate, that will act as a hydration
barrier causing a longer induction period, since water must diffuse through the carbonate
into the oxide interface before rapid hydration takes place [Song and Kim, 1990]. Chen
et al. [2002] refers to this phenomenon as improving the hydration resistance. As a result,
the expectation is that the expansive reaction happening from hydration will be postponed
considerably and the shrinkage compensation potential utilized more, and these views
form the foundation for the examinations made in this thesis.
Other studies have examined carbonation of MgO, but these do not focus on carbonating
free MgO in an atmosphere of pure CO2. Torres­Rodríguez and Pfeiffer [2011] includes
carbonation in the presence of water vapor, while Fernández et al. [1999] examines the
carbonation of MgO slurries. This aroused the interest in investigating carbonation of
free MgO in an atmosphere of pure CO2, with the intention of increasing its hydration
resistance to examine the effect of skin carbonation on autogenous deformation.
Experiments have later shown that the conditions applied to the experimental setup can
not lead to a carbonation of MgO with any practical interest. A reasonable explanation
is given by Lackner et al. [1997], that describe how the carbonation reaction of free MgO
during atmospheric pressure is far too slow. This clarifies the use of water vapor or slurries
for the carbonation experiments in the other studies by Fernández et al. [1999] & Torres­
Rodríguez and Pfeiffer [2011]. This information has then led focus on examining free CaO
instead as expansive additive to mitigate the autogenous shrinkage.
As research show promising results in improving the hydration resistance of free CaO
from skin carbonation, this may be suggested for retarding the expansive reaction from
hydration, so the concerns about CaO hydrating before setting may be prevented. Know­
ing the effectiveness of free CaO as shrinkage reducing additives, carbonating the oxide
may help controlling the initial point of hydration even better, and perhaps lead to a more
efficient shrinkage compensation with higher utilization of the CaO. This is the foundation
of the experiments made in this thesis.
6
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:17==
3
Theory
3.1
Hydration
For cement to acquire its strength and adhesive properties, it must be mixed with wa­
ter. This chemical reaction is commonly referred to as hydration of cement, which results
in products with the setting and hardening characteristics desired [Mehta and Monteiro,
2014]p.447−448.
When mixing cement with water, it produces a workable paste that is able to deform plas­
tically. This stage is called the dormant stage. For some time, the cement paste will
remain workable until a certain stage where it begins to stiffen to such a degree that it
becomes unworkable, yet is still soft. This is the start of the setting stage, the so­called
initial set, and the time from water addition until this stage is the initial setting time. While
the cement continues to hydrate, the paste becomes more stiff during the setting period
until it has developed a skeleton of rigid solid. This is known as the final set, and the
time till here is the final setting time. From this stage the paste will harden and gain its
characteristic strength over time [Soroka, 1979]p.28. The actual points of initial­ and final
setting are not fixed and may vary on the chemical composition of the cement as well
as additives, and are also very dependent on the w/c­ratio and curing conditions. This is
discussed more thoroughly in Section 7.6 including methods to determine the setting time
phenomenon. The various stages of the setting and hardening of a cement paste from
hydration is shown in Figure 3.1.
Plastic and 
workable paste
Stiff and 
unworkable paste
Rigid solid gaining 
strength with time
Final set
Initial set
Addition of water
Initial setting 
time
Final setting 
time
Setting
Dormant period
Hardening
Figure 3.1: Schematic description of setting and hardening of a cement paste [Soroka, 1979]p.28.
During hydration, the microstructure of the cement paste changes. When water is added
to cement, the anhydrous cement compounds are dissolved into their ionic constituents.
Portland cement is composed of a heterogeneous mixture of several clinker compounds,
so the hydration process of these compounds occur simultaneously. However, not all
compounds hydrate with the same rate. The aluminates (C3A and C4AF) will hydrate at
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
7
==PAGE:18==
a much faster rate than the silicates (C3S and C2S); as a result, ettringite is formed on
the surface of the cement particles at an early stage for later to become monosulfate.
The amount of aluminates and their hydration reactions will determine the stiffening and
setting characteristics of the cement paste.
When the cement paste has reached setting and starts to harden, it is the hydration of
the silicates forming C­S­H (calcium silicate hydrate) and Ca(OH)2. C­S­H is a common
notation of the hydrates formed, since it varies depending on several factors, so it does
not imply a fixed chemical composition. The hydration rate of the silicates are vital for the
hardening characteristics.
The microstructure formed from hydration is therefore dependent on the cement compo­
sition as well as other additives. The hydrates’ crystal form is needle­like which has a
large volume of fine pores. This affects the degree of self­desiccation and thereby the au­
togenous deformation, why the microstructure formed also is of importance [Mehta and
Monteiro, 2014]p.201−208.
3.2
Autogenous deformation
Autogenous deformation is the external deformation of the body of cement paste, and ’au­
togenous’ means self­generated, implying that no external factors affect the deformation
and is only a result of the hydration of the cement [Jensen, 2005]p.13−14. The mechanisms
that causes this phenomenon will be described throughout this section.
When cement hydrates its reaction products occupy a smaller space than the reacting
materials, leading to a volume reduction. This includes both the liquid and solid state
of hydration mentioned above. This phenomenon is called chemical shrinkage [Tazawa,
1998]p.3+13. However, when the cement starts to set and form a rigid solid it becomes ca­
pable of resisting shrinkage compared to the dormant state, making volume reduction from
further hydration being compensated by the formation of internal voids in the microstruc­
ture. These internal voids will result in formation of water menisci in the pore structure,
that reduces the water vapor pressure by drying out, leading to a decrease in relative hu­
midity (RH). This phenomenon is called self­desiccation, since the RH decreases in the
hardening cement body without any exchange of matter with the surroundings. The ability
of free water to exist at equilibrium with the atmosphere of certain RH depends on the pore
size. As the pore sizes decrease due to hydration and formation of the microstructure, as
well as the amount of free water reduces, the tendency to more self­desiccation occur.
As a result, capillary tensile stresses increase and the microstructure tensions leading to
an external shrinkage of the cement paste called self­desiccation shrinkage [Jensen and
Hansen, 1996, 2001; Tazawa, 1998].
Thus, self­desiccation shrinkage is a constituent part of autogenous deformation as a
result of RH­decrease from chemical shrinkage. Autogenous deformation is therefore
commonly known as autogenous shrinkage.
The self­desiccation shrinkage is a result of contraction of the rigid cement body due to
self­desiccation as a result of hydration of the cementitious materials. The magnitude of
self­desiccation shrinkage is therefore particularly dependent on the composition of the
cement mix. A stronger rigid cement body will be more resistant to contraction, why it
may be expected, that lower w/c­ratios will lead to a lower shrinkage, yet this increases
the chemical shrinkage. However, it is known that addition of aggregates lead to a bet­
ter restraint of the cement paste reducing the shrinkage, why autogenous deformation
is lower for concrete than cement paste [Neville, 2011]p.426. The addition of silica fume
has proved to increase the autogenous shrinkage considerably, since its chemical shrink­
age produced from hydration may be up to four times larger than that of typical portland
8
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:19==
cement. Furthermore, the addition of silica fume will result in a finer pore structure of
the cement paste, increasing self­desiccation and self­desiccation shrinkage [Jensen and
Hansen, 1996]. It is therefore a rather complex phenomenon to predict the magnitude of
the autogenous shrinkage from self­desiccation shrinkage.
Another influence on the autogenous deformation is crystallization of salts. This will have
an influence on the formation of microstructure and thereby the pore structure. When
reacting with water, the salt particles increase in size and occupy more space in the pores,
which force the rigid solid to expand. However, due to the lack of salts in paste with normal
portland cement, the autogenous deformation is usually governed by the self­desiccation
shrinkage [Jensen, 1993].
Although autogenous deformation is the apparent volume deformation, it is usually ex­
pressed as a linear strain [Tazawa, 1998; Neville, 2011]p.3and426. This requires a technique
that measures linear deformation, as used in this thesis, mentioned in Section 4.4. The
magnitude of the autogenous deformation can thereby only be determined properly after
the final setting time, introducing the time zero problematic, that discuss the setting time
phenomena, which is also discussed in Section 7.6.
As mentioned previously, no external factors influence the autogenous deformation; The
conditions applied to autogenous deformation therefore imply a closed, isothermal sys­
tem not subjected to any external forces. A closed system means, that the hydrating
cementitious materials will have no interference with the surroundings, most importantly
no exchange of water, since this will make a change in relative humidity (RH), and may
lead to drying shrinkage. The isothermal system signifies a constant temperature during
the hydration process. When the system is not subjected to any external forces, it is un­
restrained from anything beside its own system and therefore able to deform freely.
Autogenous deformation do take place even though the autogenous conditions do not ap­
ply. In this case, the total deformation is not entirely a result of autogenous deformation,
making it difficult to identify the magnitude of the autogenous phenomena when not sub­
jected to the autogenous conditions. This is why autogenous conditions are a necessity
when measuring the autogenous deformation [Jensen and Hansen, 2001].
3.3
Hydration of free CaO and ­MgO
Several studies about compensation for the autogenous deformation by using expansive
additives such as free CaO and MgO are proven to have a significant effect [Collepardi
et al., 2005; Min et al., 1995; Mo et al., 2012, 2015a,b; Polat et al., 2015; Seo et al.,
2020]. The mechanism for compensation of autogenous deformation by the use of free
CaO and MgO will be described in this section. When CaO and MgO hydrate, they form
calcium hydroxide (Ca(OH)2) and magnesium hydroxide (Mg(OH)2), respectively, as seen
in equations (3.1) and (3.2).
CaO + H2O ⇀
↽Ca(OH)2
(3.1)
MgO + H2O ⇀
↽Mg(OH)2
(3.2)
The products Ca(OH)2 and Mg(OH)2 from hydration will occupy a larger volume than free
CaO and MgO. But when the free water in the system is consumed from hydration, the
volume is also reduced due to chemical shrinkage. The reduction in volume of the paste
from consumption of free water and hydrated free CaO and ­MgO taking up more space
as a result of hydration, may eventually lead to an expansion in the system [Soroka, 1979].
This is because the formed cement body from hydration suddenly becomes capable of
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
9
==PAGE:20==
restraining the expansive reactions, that will increase the internal compression towards
the other particles and result in expansion of the system. The admixture of CaO and MgO
may therefore cause a sort of chemically prestressed cement paste [Collepardi et al.,
2005].
It is important to emphasize, that it is the formation of Ca(OH)2 and Mg(OH)2 as a result of
hydration of free CaO and MgO, that causes the expansion, because the product Ca(OH)2
is also formed during hydration of cement clinker minerals, which is not considered to
cause expansion. Min et al. [1995] have studied the mechanism of expansion in hardened
cement pastes with hard­burnt free CaO and state that the Ca(OH)2 crystals produced
from hydration of cement clinker minerals disperse throughout the system and are not
confined. Hence, the growth of them do not contribute to any expansion. The Ca(OH)2
particles from hydration of free CaO do not try to swell up the pore space, because they are
restricted by OH−ions in the pore solutions and concentrated in cramped regions near the
surfaces of the lime grains. For the hydration product of free CaO to increase in volume,
the Ca(OH)2 formed will unavoidably enclose many voids which will act as a solid, why the
solid volume is increased. So as a result of hydration of free CaO, the apparent volume
will increase, but the absolute volume may decrease from chemical shrinkage. After a
certain period of hydration, the crystals of Ca(OH)2 formed by hydration of free CaO will
eventually come into contact with the surrounding hydrates building up an expansive force,
that will lead to expansion [Min et al., 1995]. This phenomena is applied to MgO as well
but has a slower reaction rate [Polat et al., 2015]. When hydration of CaO is mentioned
further on in this thesis, it is referring to hydration of free CaO and not hydration of the
CaO in the cement clinker minerals.
3.3.1
Particles
Like cement, the particles of CaO and MgO vary in size and shape, which can be shown
as a particle size distribution. This distribution will show the average size of the particles
as well as the volume of different particle sizes.
Another way to characterize the particles is by fineness. The fineness relates to the spe­
cific surface area [cm2/g] of the particles. This represents both the size and shape of the
particles.
The size and shape of the particles of CaO and MgO are of great importance when con­
sidering its hydration rate and expansive reaction. Regarding hydration rate, smaller par­
ticles may hydrate faster than larger particles, due to the higher specific surface area.
Conversely, the larger particles may produce a larger expansion than that of smaller par­
ticles due to the larger volume [Polat et al., 2015].
3.4
Carbonation
In cement production a huge part is producing burned limestone (CaO). This is done by
heating limestone (CaCO3) at very high temperatures to get rid of the CO2, also called
calcination. It is a reversible reaction as seen in (3.3), meaning that the equilibrium can be
shifted in each direction. The point of equilibrium is dependent on the conditions applied to
the reaction, including pressure and temperature in the system. At standard pressure (p =
1 atm), the temperature in the system will determine which product is thermodynamically
favored. Since studies show, that the hydration resistance of CaO can be improved by
skin carbonation [Song and Kim, 1990; Chen et al., 2002], it is of interest to identify the
temperature from where CaCO3 is thermodynamically favored. When the temperature is
below the point of equilibrium with an atmosphere of enough CO2 for CaO to react with,
then the carbonation process can proceed. The temperature where the equilibrium has
10
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:21==
occurred can be determined theoretically from the thermodynamic equilibrium equation
seen in (3.4) [Hansen, 1995]p.5.12.
CaCO3(s) ⇀
↽CaO(s) + CO2(g)
(3.3)
∆rGθ
T = −R · T · ln(K)
(3.4)

J/(mol·K) is the gas constant and K is the thermodynamic equilibrium constant. ∆rGθ

dependent on the enthalpy and entropy and can be calculated from (3.5).

The standard molar reaction enthalpy, ­entropy and ­heat capacity at standard conditions
(T = 25 °C and p = 1 atm) are calculated from the change in enthalpy, entropy or heat
capacity for a chemical reaction, see equation (3.8)
∆rXθ =

(3.8)
where X is either H298, S298 or cp for the given chemical reaction. As for the reaction in
(3.3), the temperature at which the reaction is in equilibrium has been calculated to 866.8
°C. This calculation can be seen in Appendix C.3. This means that when heating CaCO3
above 866.8 °C, it will be decomposed to CaO and CO2­gas. This also means, that when
below 866.8 °C, CaO is able to react with CO2 and carbonate. In other words, CaCO3 is
thermodynamically favored when the temperature is kept below this point of equilibrium
in an atmosphere of CO2. The point of equilibrium has been calculated to 305.3 °C for
MgO.
The rate of carbonation is dependent on the temperature at which the reaction takes place.
This is proved from experiments by Chen et al. [2002], that show increase in carbonation
with increasing time and temperature until a certain temperature point.

4.1.1
Cement
The cement used in this thesis is the Aalborg White® from Aalborg Portland. It has the
cement designation CEM I 52,5 N SR5. Material properties can be seen in Table 4.1. A
Product­ and Safety Data Sheet can be seen in Appendix D.3.
4.1.2
CaO
The calcium oxide (CaO) used in the experiments comes from Lhoist and is lightly burned
powdered lime and has been manufactured by burning crushed limestone with pulver­
ization afterwards. The burning reaches temperatures within 1100­1200 °C to assure
complete calcination for CO2 removal. Product­ and Safety Data Sheets can be seen in
Appendix D.1 and properties can be seen in Table 4.1. The CaO has sieve residues at
90 µm of 10% ± 5%. The material is stored in an enclosed container, unexposed for the
atmosphere. When mentioning CaO or untreated CaO throughout the thesis, it is meant
as the amount of CaO powder taken from the container and not of the 95% CaO that the
sample contains, unless specified otherwise.
4.1.3
MgO
The magnesia (MgO) used in this thesis has been shipped from China. The components
in the magnesia and its properties can be seen in Table 4.1. The material is stored in
an enclosed container, so it is unexposed from the atmosphere. When mentioning MgO
throughout the thesis, it is meant as the amount of MgO powder taken from the container
and not of the 90.05% MgO that the material contains. Data sheets containing some other
characteristics can be seen in Appendix D.2.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials

==PAGE:24==
4.1.4
Silica fume
The silica fume used for the castings is the Elkem Microsilica® Grade 940 for fibre cement.
Physically it acts as a filler and chemically as a highly reactive pozzolan, containing more
than 90% SiO2. The Safety Data Sheets can be seen in Appendix D.5.
4.1.5
Superplasticizer
The superplasticizer used in the castings are the SPT 100. It is produced by Giovanni
Bozzetto and in conformity to EN 934­2.2009+A1 2012. SPT 100 is stated as a high
range water reducing plasticizing admixture for concrete; EN 934­2:T3.1/3.2. Product­
and Safety Data Sheets can be seen in Appendix D.4.
4.1.6
Aggregates
Sand is used for some castings to illustrate its mechanical influence on autogenous defor­
mation. It is danish sand in the size of 0­2 mm. However, during experiments it is crushed
into smaller sizes for comparison of its fineness, mentioned in Section 4.7.4.
4.2
Mix design
The mix design aims for a w/c­ratio of 0.30. Beside cement and water, it contains silica
fume (SF), which is set to 20% of the cement weight. For appropriate mixing, it contains
1% superplasticizer (SPC) of the entire powder weight, which includes cement, silica fume
and optionally expansive additive. The expansive additives used in the castings are MgO
and CaO. The MgO is added as x% of the powder weight of cement and SF, while the
CaO added is taken as x% of the powder weight of cement, SF and itself. The x% addition
refers to the variation of amount of expansive additive included in the castings. The spec­
imens cast with different amounts of additive can be seen in Table 6.3. All the castings
with a complete mix design can be seen in Appendix C.1.

4.3
Casting
The cement paste mixes are cast based on DS/EN 196­1:2016 [2016] but a more thorough
procedure is described in Appendix A.1. For mixing, a bowl, blade and mixer is needed to
prepare the cement paste for moulding. Before water is added to the mix, the cementitious
materials are stirred together to make a proper and homogeneous binder, see Figure
14
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:25==
4.1a. The time from where water is added is the initial time for the hydration process.
The mix is stirred continuously, and total mixing from where water is added lasts around
5 minutes before the paste is poured into the mould. The moulds for measurements
in the dilatometer can be seen in Figure 4.1c and moulds for the manual deformation
measurements can be seen in Figure 4.1b. When moulding, vibration is necessary to
assure a complete filling of the moulds.
4.4
Autogenous deformation
The autogenous deformation will be measured in two ways; By dilatometer and by a man­
ual measuring gauge. The dilatometer method is the primary method used for autogenous
deformation measurements according to ASTM C 1698 [2009], where the specimens will
be kept at a constant temperature of 30 °C in this thesis. The measuring gauge, or also
referred to as tube method, is used for measuring the specimens kept at 20 °C but the
specimens are stored differently than the guidelines according to ASTM C 1698 [2009].
4.4.1
Dilatometer
To measure the autogenous strain of cement with the dilatometer method, freshly mixed
paste will be poured into a corrugated mold with little resistance to length change, seen
in Figure 4.1c. The corrugated mold is sealed in both ends to assure an efficient sealing
of the cement paste. The length of the moulds are by default 300 mm, unless otherwise
specified. The deformation measurements are performed in a dilatometer by transducers
that show an insignificant restraint of the cement paste. During measurements of the
deformation, the dilatometer is submerged in a thermostatically controlled glycol bath set
to 30 °C. Altogether, complete autogenous conditions are almost reached, meaning the
deformation measured represents the autogenous deformation [Jensen, 1995]. Figure
4.2b shows the sealed specimens in the dilatometer.
(a)

bath
with

specimens at a time.

sion into the glycol bath.
Figure 4.2: Experimental setup for autogenous deformation measurements according to ASTM C
1698.
4.4.2
Tube method
This measuring technique was developed due to concerns about the dilatometer, ex­
pressed in Section 7.3. The specimens for the tube method are cast in moulds seen
in Figure 4.1b and are by default 285x25x25 mm. The specimens are measured manu­
ally, so the are cast with a set screw in each end, so the specimen can be measured by
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials

the measuring gauge when demoulded, see Figure 4.4a. The manual measuring gauge
is seen in Figure 4.3.
(a) Specimen placed in measuring gauge.
(b) Close­up of measuring gauge and specimen end
with set screw.
Figure 4.3: Experimental setup for manual deformation measurements.
Storage of the specimens has to be considered carefully, so the autogenous conditions
are still complied with; The constant temperature was kept by placing the specimens in
a room set at 20 °C. An efficient sealing and no external restraint of the specimen are
also required. An alumina tube was cut for the specimen to be placed in. This way, the
specimen is only restrained by its own friction towards the side of the alumina tube, and
only in contact at the edges of the specimen, see Figure 4.4b and 4.4c. Rubber plugs are
then used for each end, so the sealing becomes efficient.

other phenomena such as drying shrinkage. Whether the deformation measured only rep­
resents the autogenous deformation will be discussed in Section 7.3, but in the section
treating the results it will be referred to as the autogenous deformation.

when reaching equilibrium with the ambient vapor [Greenspan, 1977].

4.6
Carbonation
For the carbonation experiments, an apparatus for thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) will
be used for initial examinations, while a tube furnace will be used for carbonating larger
samples.
4.6.1
Thermogravimetric analysis (TGA)
The preliminary examinations of CaO and MgO and their carbonation characteristics are
investigated by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA). The TGA is made using a Netzsch STA
449 F3 Jupiter with the purpose of either calcining or carbonating a specimen. The initial
analyses consist of calcining CaCO3 and MgCO3 to identify the point of equilibrium from
where it is decomposed into CaO and MgO and compare with the theoretical calculations
seen in section 3.4. When calcining a specimen, its weight is measured as a function of
temperature going from 20 °C to 1000 °C in a non­reactive atmosphere of constant N2­
gas flow. The point where the decomposition rate is highest is then taken as the point of
equilibrium.
The carbonation process includes pre­heating of the material to a chosen temperature
below the point of equilibrium where the carbonation process will proceed. During pre­
heating, the atmosphere is kept non­reactive with N2­gas. When the desired temperature
has been reached, the atmosphere of N2­gas is kept for a short duration of time (30 min) to
ensure, that the material and scale have been stabilized from the increase in temperature.
After the stabilization, the experiment runs for a chosen time with CO2 as atmosphere to
measure the weight increase from carbonation.
The heating rate is set at a standard of 20 K/min, but calcining experiments have been run
with both 5 and 10 K/min as well. The flow of the surrounding atmosphere was initially set
to 50 mL/min, however results later showed, that this had to be increased to 120 mL/min
for the CO2 to react with the specimen. This led up to doubts about the apparatus working
correctly, since calculations show that the stoichiometry has excess of CO2, see Appendix
C.4. These concerns are expressed in Appendix A.2 including a more thorough procedure
description.
4.6.2
Carbonation by tube furnace
When carbonating CaO in larger portions, another setup is needed. In this thesis, the
skin carbonated samples are produced in a tube furnace. The tube assures a steady
flow across the specimen from an inlet gas of choice, in this case both N2 and CO2 are
used as for the TGA. This tube is made of Al2O3 and is sealed at both ends only with a
small hole in each end being the inlet and outlet of the chosen atmosphere. The crucibles
placed in the tube furnace containing the CaO samples are likewise made of Al2O3, the
data sheet can be seen in Appendix D.6. The gas flow is set to 2.5 NL/min (normal liters
per minute) to ensure that the sample is not ’starving’ of CO2 as well as a quick cleansing
of the atmosphere when the gas is changed. The temperature of which the carbonation
is taking place varies between the experiments, but is kept under the point of equilibrium,
where CaCO3 is favored thermodynamically. The procedure for carbonating the larger
samples are described in Appendix A.3.
18
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:29==
(a) Tube furnace showing the inlet mounted to the tube.
(b) Crucible shown inside the tube where
the inlet has been taken off.
Figure 4.6: Experimental setup for carbonation of CaO samples.
4.7
Particle characterization
As mentioned in Section 3.3.1 the particles of cement­based materials can be character­
ized by different parameters. The parameters examined in this thesis are particle size
distribution and specific surface area.
4.7.1
Laser diffraction
The particle size distribution is determined from laser diffraction according to DS/ISO
13320:2020. The apparatus used is a Scirocco 2000 from Malvern instruments, it can
be seen in Figure 4.7a.
(a) Apparatus Scirocco 2000 from Malvern instruments used for
laser diffraction.
(b) Air permeability method (Blaine
method).
Figure 4.7: Experimental setup for determining the particle size distribution (a) and specific surface
area (b).
4.7.2
Air permeability method (Blaine method)
The specific surface area can be determined by air permeability. The method used is
called the Blaine method and is done according to DS/EN 196­6:2018. The principle is
to measure the fineness of the cement­based material by observing the time taken for a
fixed amount of air to flow through a compacted bed of the material of known dimensions
and porosity. An experimental setup can be seen in Figure 4.7b.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
19
==PAGE:30==
From the Blaine method, the specific surface, S, is found by (4.1):
S = K
ρ ·
√
e3
(1 −e) ·
√
t
√10 · η
(4.1)
where K is the apparatus constant determined from (4.2);
e is the porosity of the bed, chosen as e =0.5;
t is the measured time in seconds;
ρ is the density of the cementitious material examined in g/cm3;
η is the viscosity of air at the test temperature. It is taken from Table 1 in DS/EN 196­
6:2018 and is expressed in Pa·s.
K = S0 · ρ0 · (1 −e) · √10 · η
√
e3 · √t0
(4.2)
where S0 is the specific surface of the reference cement in cm2/g;
ρ0 is the density of the reference cement in g/cm3;
t0 is the mean of the three measured times in seconds;
η0 is the air viscosity at the mean of the three temperatures in Pa·s.
4.7.3
Particle sieving
Sieving is necessary for dividing the particles into different fractions for further investi­
gations of the shrinkage compensation effect. The procedure can be seen in Appendix
A.6. A setup for sieving can be seen in Figure 4.8 also showing a pestle and mortar for
separation of the agglomerated particles.
Figure 4.8: Setup for sieving CaO and skin carbonated CaO into different fractions.
4.7.4
Ball mill grinding
For crushing of the sand into smaller particles, the ball mill grinding method is used. The
sand is put in a container together with marble balls in different sizes that will hit each other
when spinned, crushing the sand into finer particles. See Figure 4.9 for the container in­
cluding the marble balls. The procedure is described more thoroughly in the experimental
description in Appendix A.5.
20
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:31==
(a) Container for aggregates and marble
balls.
(b) Appropriate amount of marble balls for
grinding.
Figure 4.9: Container including marble balls for grinding the sand into finer particles.
4.8
Setting time
The final setting time also known as Time zero, T0, used for the autogenous deforma­
tion plots will be determined a little untraditionally from the well­known Vicat test. It will
be determined by the deformation rate of the autogenous deformation courses for each
specimen, and for comparison, the temperature development for some specimens are
observed.
4.8.1
Determined by deformation curves
This method is used to determine the final setting time for the autogenous deformation
specimens measured by dilatometer method. The final setting time is taken as the point
from where the largest shrinkage rate is observed before the deformation rate becomes
steady. The method and its credibility is discussed in Section 7.6.
4.8.2
Temperature development
The temperature development is analyzed for some specimens by measuring the temper­
ature inside the specimen. The point from where the temperature experience a significant
increase is taken as the final setting time. This point is found by the interception between
linear regression fits of the two parts of the temperature development. They have been
divided into these two parts by the k­means clustering method, that divides the data set
into two parts that has the best fit.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
21
==PAGE:32==
4.9
Scanning electron microscope (SEM)
It is expected that the surface of the CaO particles may have changed due to carbonation
or hydration, and a way to investigate this is by using a scanning electron microscope
(SEM). With SEM an inspection of the particles down to microscopic level can be made
in order of both examining particle size and particle structure. The materials examined
are CaO and skin carbonated CaO with a DOC of 21.5% and 10.2%. To compare with
characteristics, both CaCO3 particles and Ca(OH)2 particles are investigated as well. The
scanning electron microscope used is a FEI Quanta 250, and the setup can be seen in
Figure 4.10.
(a) FEI Quanta 250 SEM apparatus.
(b) Specimens placed in dilatometer before submer­
sion into the glycol bath.
Figure 4.10: Experimental setup for scanning electron microscope.

Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials

5
Examinations of MgO
The intention with this thesis was to examine MgO as expansive additive for autoge­
nous shrinkage compensation. Skin carbonating MgO was expected to retard its expan­
sive reaction, leading to a more efficient shrinkage compensation. The experiments later
showed, that carbonation of MgO is not possible for the experimental setup available, why
further investigation of MgO is not of interest. Another reason for this was, that the au­
togenous deformation measurements did not seem to make use of a retarded expansive
reaction. The experimental process is described in this chapter including the results from
the experiments with carbonation of MgO as well as its effect on autogenous shrinkage.
5.1
Specimens
For examining the effect of MgO as expansive additive, specimens are made for defor­
mation measurements. The mix design aimed for a w/c­ratio of 0.30, however due to the
rheology having a high viscosity, the w/c­ratio was increased to 0.36. As mentioned in
Section 4.2, the mix design contains cement, silica fume (20% of cement weight), super­
plasticizer (1% of powder weight) and water. The MgO is added as x% of the powder
weight of cement and SF, with the x% addition referring to the variation of amount of MgO
included in the specimens. The specimens made for the deformation experiments can be
seen in Table 5.1. All the specimen mix designs can be seen in Appendix C.1.
Table 5.1: List of castings made for evaluating the effect on autogenous deformation of MgO.
Specimen name
Additive
Ref­0.36*

MgO­8%

MgO­10%*
10% MgO100

*The specimens’ temperature development has been measured for discussion of the setting time,

The amounts of MgO added for examining the shrinkage compensation were chosen to
investigate the influence of large amounts added. Furthermore, other studies have used
up to 8% MgO as additive before seeing a significant compensation [Mo et al., 2012,
2015a].
5.2
Carbonation
Experiments with carbonation of MgO were made. Carbonation of MgO was first exam­
ined by TGA. Initially, calcination of MgCO3 was made to identify the point of equilibrium
from where CO2 would be decomposed from MgCO3 to MgO. This temperature is found
by analyzing the rate of mass change, with the largest change indicating the temperature.
In Figure 5.1, the calcining of MgCO3 is shown, and using this analysis procedure the
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
23
==PAGE:34==
temperature is found to T = 517.23 °C. However, the calcining process extends over a
long temperature span from 150 °C to 600 °C, so the exact point may be uncertain and
difficult to determine. The corresponding theoretical point of equilibrium was calculated
to T = 305.33 °C, see Appendix C.3. This lead to concerns about the possibility of skin
carbonating MgO, since it does not seem to have a specific temperature from where the
existence of MgCO3 is favored in a reaction between MgO and CO2.

For the further investigations of the possibility to carbonate MgO, a sample was exposed
for the CO2 atmosphere at 100 °C since the calcination took place from around 150 °C.
This can be seen in Figure 5.2 showing the degree of carbonation [%] as a function of
time [h]. It is clear, that the carbonation process is not steady and only carbonate around
1%.
After several attempts no considerable sign of carbonation took place. This led to an
investigation of the carbonation process of MgO at different temperature steps for one
sample, seen in Figure 5.3. The sample was exposed to temperature steps varying from
150 °C to 350 °C all for two hours, only showing a mass loss when the temperature in­
creased and what seems to be loss on ignition when the temperature is kept constant.
This supports the concern about the possibility to skin carbonate MgO. A study examin­
ing carbonation of MgO proved that MgO was unable to achieve reasonable carbonation
kinetics during atmospheric pressure (1 atm) [Lackner et al., 1997]. This means that the
conditions applied to the TGA are not suitable for carbonating MgO.

the MgO to retard its conversion to Mg(OH)2, because this reaction is what makes the
expansion for shrinkage compensation.

5.3
Autogenous deformation
The effect of MgO as shrinkage reducing additive on the autogenous deformation has
been examined. The autogenous deformation of the specimens containing varying amount
of MgO can be seen in Figure 5.4. The reference casting of w/c­ratio = 0.36 is shown for
comparison. The deformation curves are plotted from the final setting time determined
by the largest shrinkage rate, also mentioned in Section 6.5 and discussed in Section
7.6. The figure shows, that MgO as additive will reduce the autogenous deformation. As
expected, it will be reduced proportionally with the amount of MgO added. This tendency
is not as clear for MgO­4%, MgO­6% and MgO­8%, that seem to reduce the shrinkage
equally at first glance. However, they each have a different slope, and if the measure­
ments had continued, the deformations were expected to differ. This confusion is probably
due to the method used for determination of setting, from where the deformation curves
are plotted. Nevertheless, the autogenous strain for the reference specimen is ­2774
µm/m and ­1915 µm/m with 10% MgO after 330 h. This can be considered a significant
reduction, but modest compared to the results obtained for CaO. The rest of the autoge­
nous strains can be seen in Table 5.2.

Regarding the shape of the deformation curves for the MgO­specimens, no particular
indication of a chemical reaction is seen, not even for the 10% MgO specimen. This led
to a more thorough analysis, examining if the reduction in shrinkage only occurred due
to a mechanical restraint by the MgO particles, meaning the MgO only will have an inert
effect on the autogenous deformation. To check for this, the autogenous deformation of
specimens containing 10% sand was measured. Initially, sand in the fraction of 0­2 mm
was used for comparison, but since the MgO­particles are much smaller, other specimens
containing crushed sand with a specific surface of 1990 and 6890 cm2/g was also added
to the comparison. The crushed sand was achieved from ball mill grinding mentioned in
Section 4.7.4 for 6 h and 24 h, respectively. The autogenous deformation can be seen in
Figure 5.5.

Figure 5.5: Autogenous deformation at 30 °C of specimens containing 10% additive and a w/c­
ratio of 0.36. The number behind the sand­specimens indicate the time of ball mill grinding in
hours. The reference casting is shown for comparison.
The figure shows, that MgO does not only affect the autogenous shrinkage mechanically,
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
27
==PAGE:38==
since it proves to reduce the shrinkage more than sand particles. This is especially seen
when compared to the autogenous deformation from Sand­6h and Sand­24h, that are
almost identical, even though their fineness differ significantly. MgO has a specific surface
area of 4550 cm2/g according to the data sheet seen in Appendix D.2, but was measured
to 4270 cm2/g with the Blaine method, that was also used for measuring the fineness of
the sand. See Section 6.2 for these results. The deformation curves illustrate, that if the
particles have a high fineness, the autogenous deformation will not be affected as much
as for particles with a much more inferior fineness such as sand 0­2 mm. This means,
that the particle fineness of additives at least has a substantial mechanical influence on
the autogenous deformation and may also have a chemical influence.
Regarding the experiments with MgO, it was expected to result in a more significant com­
pensation of the autogenous shrinkage. According to a study by Mo et al. [2015a], port­
land cement pastes containing 8% MgO proved to decrease the autogenous shrinkage
by around 40% after only 120 h stored at 23± 1 °C. This paste did not contain 20% silica
fume, so it cannot be compared directly with the measurements made in this thesis, but
there were higher expectations for the magnesia’s ability to compensate for the autoge­
nous shrinkage. Mo et al. [2012] have made examinations for a longer period, showing
that expansion by MgO may first appear after 20 days for a 8% MgO portland cement
paste assumed to cure at 30 °C. This indicates, that measurements over a longer period
must apply for the experiments made in this thesis before observing any expansion. The
expectation of an improved utilization of MgO for shrinkage compensation by skin car­
bonating the MgO beforehand may therefore not apply, and skin carbonation will then be
irrelevant.
5.4
Conclusion
This thesis intended to examine MgO as expansive additive to compensate for autoge­
nous shrinkage. It was imagined that carbonation of MgO was possible for increasing
the hydration resistance to retard its expansive reaction from hydration, leading to a more
efficient shrinkage compensation. Yet, the examinations proved, that under the experi­
mental circumstances, carbonation was not possible. In addition, the deformation curves
of the specimen containing MgO did not express any sign of expansive reaction happen­
ing too early, why a skin carbonation was found unnecessary. Although, the shrinkage
compensation by the use of MgO improved with the amount of MgO added. Altogether, it
was decided to continue the examinations with CaO, since this showed more promising
and interesting results regarding skin carbonation and magnitudes in autogenous defor­
mation.
28
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:39==
6
Results and analyses
6.1
Carbonation
6.1.1
Preliminary examinations
The materials’ ability to carbonate were examined by the use of TGA. The conditions
applied to TGA are complete sealing meaning that the specimen can be isolated by the
chosen atmosphere of atmospheric pressure (p = 1 atm). Before carbonating the CaO
samples, preliminary examinations of the material were made. Initially, calcination of pure
CaCO3 were conducted to find the point of equilibrium between CaCO3 and CaO, although
calculations of this point has been stated in Section 3.4.
The temperature at which the point of equilibrium is reached is found by analyzing the rate
of mass change, with the largest change indicating the temperature. For calcination of
CaCO3, the temperature is found to T = 821.24 °C. This can easily be seen in Figure 6.1,
showing the mass of CaCO3 in % as a function of temperature with the rate of change in
%/min shown as well.

is found to T = 821.24 °C.
Depending on the heating rate, the temperature of the point of equilibrium is shifted, with
increasing heating rate leading to a higher temperature point. This is also seen in Figure
6.2 showing the calcination of CaCO3. The temperatures for the point of equilibrium for a
heating rate of 5 °C/min, 10 °C/min and 20 °C/min is found to T = 782.31 °C, T = 791.19 °C
and as mentioned before T = 821.24 °C, respectively. The theoretical point of equilibrium
has been calculated to T = 866.8 °C. This deviates from the experiments performed, but

From the preliminary examinations, several attempts of skin carbonating specimens of
CaO and MgO were made at temperatures of which carbonation of the oxides are ther­
modynamically favored. The samples were run at different temperatures to observe the
liability to convert the oxides into carbonates. For CaO, the temperature at which car­
bonation can take place, is assured to be below the point of equilibrium. A plot of the
degree of carbonation (DOC) as a function of time at different temperatures can be seen
in Figure 6.3. Only a selection of the experiments are shown, since problems occurred
when using the TGA apparatus. It clearly shows, that with a flow rate of 50 mL CO2
per minute, no carbonation takes place. However, calculations have been made proving
the reaction can proceed and that the stoichiometry shows excess of CO2, see Appendix
C.4. This led to doubts about the credibility of the carbonation results achieved from the
TGA. The CO2 flow rate was then increased to 120 mL/min improving the DOC signifi­
cantly. Nonetheless, the carbonation curves are still unsteady, and some samples even
lose weight, implying that something is wrong with the measurements. This is especially
seen for the DOC curve at 400 °C and 610 °C. If the results are reliable, carbonating CaO
at 510 °C for 10 hours will result in a skin carbonated specimen of around 8%, which is
the aim for the further carbonation of samples used for shrinkage compensation.

6.1.2
Carbonated specimens
After the preliminary examinations, larger samples were carbonated by a tube furnace as
mentioned in Section 4.6.2. Since the carbonation curves indicated measurements with
questionable results, a test carbonation of CaO was made. The test was set to run at
510 °C, however it is rather difficult to reach and keep the desired temperature in the tube
furnace, since the temperature profile along the tube is not constant. The test was run with
CO2 for 13 h and with a mean temperature of 502 °C it reached a degree of carbonation
of 24.0%. Afterwards, other carbonation tests were run to produce more skin carbonated
CaO. The temperature, time and corresponding degree of carbonation can be seen in
Table 6.1. The table shows both the degree of carbonation after the sample was heated
in the tube furnace and a check of the degree of carbonation afterwards. The 13.7% skin
carbonated sample was attempted reproduced to secure enough material for the further
examinations of CaO with the same degree of carbonation. The loss on ignition during
heating has been taken into account as a function of temperature exposure. The LOI
model can be seen in Appendix B.1.

6.2
Particle size
The particle size of the different materials and samples has been examined by two meth­
ods, that represents the size of the particles and fineness. The particle sizes have been
investigated by laser diffraction, that illustrates the volume of the different particle sizes
for each material and its average particle size. The fineness is evaluated in the material’s
specific surface area, which represents the area of material exposed per material mass.
6.2.1
Particle size distribution
The particle size distribution of all the materials and samples used in the specimens was
supposed to be found by laser diffraction analysis, mentioned in Section 4.7. Due to
technical issues, not all particle size distributions have been achieved.
The particle size distribution for untreated CaO can be seen in Figure 6.4. It is seen,
that a large amount constitute the particle size above 90 µm, corresponding to around
27%. This seem unreliable, since the data sheet of CaO indicates, that the CaO only
has sieve residues at 90 µm of 10% ± 5%. Furthermore, the CaO has been sieved
and the analysis shows to have such low amount of CaO above 90 µm, that autogenous
deformation measurement of this fraction has not been made. So this leads to doubts
about the results obtained.
32
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:43==
Figure 6.4: Particle size distribution of untreated CaO.
The two skin carbonated CaO samples with DOC of 16.2% and 21.5% have also been
analyzed by laser diffraction. The particle size distributions can be seen in Figure 6.5
and 6.6. In these figures, the particle size distributions do not seem to differ that much.
This can indicate, that the DOC has a small influence on the particle sizes. However,
these distributions cannot be completely relied on as for the untreated CaO particle size
distribution.
Figure 6.5: Particle size distribution of 16.2% skin carbonated CaO.

Figure 6.6: Particle size distribution of 21.5% skin carbonated CaO.
After the laser diffraction apparatus was fixed, a test run has been made with the 13.7%
skin carbonated sample with the fraction below 40 µm. By analyzing this sample, it is
known, that the particles are smaller than 40 µm, so this is expected to be seen. The
particle size distribution is shown in Figure 6.7. It shows, that around 94% of the particles
are smaller than 40 µm. It still indicate, that some particles are larger, however this seem
to be more reliable.

A manual particle size distribution has been made for the 13.7% skin carbonated CaO,
which is the sample used for further analysis of the particle size influence. The sample
has been sieved by the sieving method mentioned in Section 4.7.3 for a duration of 5
minutes for each fraction; 90 µm and 40 µm. The three fractions then used for further
investigations are < 40 µm, 40­90 µm and > 90 µm which hs been found to constitute of
16%, 56% and 28% respectively.
6.2.2
Specific surface area
The specific surface area of the different materials have been found from the Blaine
method mentioned in Section 4.7.2, listed in Table 6.2. The different sand samples are
used for examination of the mechanical effect on autogenous deformation of the expan­
sive additives, and for a reasonable comparison, they have been grinded for different du­
rations to achieve different specific surfaces. Only the sand samples grinded for 6h and
24h have been used for casting. The skin carbonated sample examined is the CaO13.7%,
which most of the experiments are based on. The specific surface is measured to 4320
cm2/g, meaning that the fineness decreases during the skin carbonation process, since
both CaO and CaCO3 both have specific surface areas of 6260 cm2/g and 7050 cm2/g,
respectively. Regarding the different particle fractions of 13.7% skin carbonated CaO,
there is a significant difference in the specific surface areas, and this is expected to be
noticed in the autogenous deformation measurements.

6.3
Hydration resistance
In this section, the hydration resistance of untreated CaO and 13.7% skin carbonated
CaO at 20 °C is investigated. Furthermore, the three particle fractions of skin carbonated
CaO is examined for better understanding of the hydration kinetics of the CaO particles;
< 40 µm, 40­90 µm and 90 µm. The degrees of hydration of all samples are determined
from the amount of material able to hydrate of the untreated CaO, which is 95% CaO and
1.6% MgO, respectively. Regarding the skin carbonated samples, they are also adjusted
for the 13.7% carbonate that are not able to hydrate.
In Figure 6.8 it is seen, that the degree of hydration (DOH) increases proportional with the
RH as well as the exposure duration. It can be seen, that untreated CaO hydrates very
fast and already after 7 days, the DOH is above 80% at 23% RH. Furthermore, it is seen,
that CaO is very vulnerable to high relative humidities, being almost fully hydrated with a
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
35
==PAGE:46==
DOH over 90% after 2 days from 75% RH and above and has already reached 90% DOH
within a day at RHs above 95%. It is seen, that a large increase in DOH happens from 1
to 2 days of exposure at 44% RH, going from 21.28% to 74.21%, which is also the case
from 44% to 65% being 74.47% hydrated for the specimen exposed for 1 day.

Figure 6.8: Degrees of hydration of untreated CaO exposed at different relative humidities at 20°C
for the indicated durations.
Regarding the 13.7% skin carbonated sample, the hydration resistance is much larger.
The skin carbonation is especially effective against exposure at low relative humidities,
showing only a degree of hydration of 9.00% after exposure at 44% RH for 30 days.
However, from 44% to 65% RH, the DOH increases significantly to 80.60% after 30 days.
In general, a big increase in DOH happens from exposures above 65% to 75% RH, and
will eventually reach full hydration at the exposure of high relative humidities. It is noticed,
that the samples will reach a DOH above 100%, which is not stoichiometrically possible,
if the samples are 13.7% skin carbonated. This is discussed in Section 7.2.
When considering the different fractions of 13.7% skin carbonated samples, they follow
the same development both over time and the relative humidities. However, the hydration
resistance increase with particle size, and this seem to be consistent until almost full
hydration is reached. This is especially seen for the fraction < 40 µm which has a DOH
of 14.69% when exposed to a RH of 44% for 30 days, compared to 8.00% DOH for the
fraction 40­90 µm and 5.95% for the fraction > 90 µm. It is seen, that all samples reach
almost complete hydration after 3 days above 95% RH, and fully hydrated after 7 days
from 85% RH, with the exception of the sample > 90 µm at 95% RH after 14 days, which
is probably due to a measuring error. As for the 13.7% skin carbonated sample containing
every fraction, it can be observed, that the larger particle fractions seem to have hydrated
more than 100%. This confusion about the results is discussed more thoroughly in Section
7.4.2.

6.4
Specimens
As mentioned in Section 4.2, the specimens for the measurements made for examination
of CaO as expansive additive all have a w/c­ratio = 0.30. The added amount of additive
is taken as the percentage of the entire powder weight including itself (cement + SF +
additive).
For investigating the effect of CaO as expansive additive, deformation measurements are
examined by different specimens. The relevant specimens can be seen in Table 6.3. The
table shows the additive and amount as well as special treatment used for the specimens.
Each specimen has been assigned an abbreviation, that will be used further on.
Initially, untreated CaO is looked into, including specimens containing different amounts
of CaO as well as different particle sizes of CaO. These broad examinations have been
done for one skin carbonated sample as well to observe the potential of skin carbonated
CaO. Furthermore, the effect of skin carbonation can be compared by adding an equal
amount of CaO for different degrees of carbonation. The intention was to add 3% CaO,
but since the loss on ignition of the different skin carbonated samples were not taken into
account when the specimens were cast, the amounts of CaO in these specimens are not
completely identical. Luckily, the amount of CaO added for the LOI corrected degrees
of carbonation are approximately the same. These skin carbonated samples are also
mentioned later on in Section 6.1.
The naming of the specimens are made as following:
”Material””Degree of carbonation””Special treatment”­”Additive in %”
So for the ”CaO­9%”, the specimen contains 9% untreated CaO. The ”CaO13.7%<40µm­
2.93%(16%)” specimen contains 13.7% skin carbonated CaO where the particle fraction
is below 40 µm, with 16% of 2.93% CaO added (corresponding to 0.47% CaO). It is
shown this way, because the percentage of the chosen CaO amount represents the subset
that this particle fraction constitutes of the entire 13.7% skin carbonated CaO particle
distribution. The only exception is the Ref­0.30 indicating the reference specimen with
w/c = 0.30.
The amounts of CaO added are based on the observations of the MgO­specimens ex­
amined in Section 5.3 as well as studies that used up to 7.5% CaO as additive before
seeing a significant effect [Polat et al., 2015]. Furthermore, large amounts are needed to
understand the potential and reaction kinetics of CaO.

6.5
Autogenous deformation
In this section, the effect on the autogenous deformation will be examined when using CaO
as shrinkage compensating additives. Initially, experiments with untreated CaO are made
in order to investigate its potential. Then, further investigations with the skin carbonated
CaO will be made. The main measurements by dilatometer experiments are performed
at 30 °C. In the experiments with the manual measurements by the tube method, the
specimens are kept at 20 °C and will be shown in Section 6.5.3.
Regarding the dilatometer measurements, the deformation of the specimens were mea­
sured almost directly after casting, meaning the deformation during the plastic state has
been measured for each specimen. The deformation curves therefore have a floating
point zero until the specimens are set. The final setting point of each specimen has been
determined from where the shrinkage rate is largest. The autogenous deformation is then
plotted from this time for each specimen. This method has been used, because the point
is easy to locate and the autogenous deformation curves produced after this point are
consistent. The method is further discussed in Section 7.6 and compared with other ex­
aminations of the setting time. The manual deformation measurements are made directly
40
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:51==
after demoulding of the specimens, why this measurement is set as the initial deforma­
tion.
The autogenous strain after 330 h for all specimens kept at 30 °C as well as the specimens
kept at 20 °C after 41­45 days have been listen in Table 6.4 for comparison.
6.5.1
Untreated CaO
The autogenous deformation for the specimens containing untreated CaO stored at 30 °C
can be seen in figure 6.13. The features observed for MgO in regards to the autogenous
deformation are observed with CaO as well, but the amount added has a larger influence
on the autogenous deformation than of MgO regarding early age development. This is
especially seen with the specimens containing 6% and 9% even showing expansion. It
seems like the 9% CaO has fully hydrated within the first 24 h, since the deformation
curve starts to decrease in strain from here, however this shows only to be temporary and
starts expanding even more after around 100 h. After 330 h (∼14 days), the specimen
has expanded 2612 µm/m and keeps expanding up to 4946 µm/m at the age of 520 h (∼
22 days) and seems to expand even further. The rest of the CaO specimens have not
expanded after 330 h having a strain of ­641, ­1875, ­2107 and ­2416 µm/m for 6%, 3%,
2% and 1% CaO addition, respectively. It is observed, that increasing the amount of CaO
added, the shrinkage will be compensated more and eventually lead to an expansion.
Furthermore, it is seen, that the development is not linear proportional with the amount
added. This proclaim, that a huge potential for shrinkage compensation is possible, since
the chemical reactions from hydration seem to be of such significance.

The first couple of hours during the autogenous deformation of untreated CaO is interest­
ing to look into, since a retardation of the hydration happening in this period is attempted
with the skin carbonation experiments. In Figure 6.14, the same specimens are plotted
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
41
==PAGE:52==
within the first 24 h of their deformation. Equally for all specimens containing CaO, the
specimens shrink before a noticeable ’bump’ occurs, which increase in size and duration
with amount of CaO added. This happens quickly around the age of 8 h from water addi­
tion of the specimens containing 1­3% CaO, while the 6% and 9% CaO specimens show
a significant expansion lasting for a longer duration. This indicate, that a certain amount
of CaO can be added to equalize the autogenous shrinkage efficiently. Furthermore, it is
seen, that the final setting time decreases with the amount of CaO added.

The mechanical effect of CaO on the autogenous deformation is examined by comparing
a specimen containing sand grinded for 24 h. The sand has a specific surface area of
6890 cm2/g compared to 6260 cm2/g for CaO. Previous results have shown, that a great
mechanical difference occur when the particle size change, seen in Figure 5.5. The au­
togenous deformation comparison can be seen in Figure 6.15 with the reference casting
shown as well. The figure shows, that CaO do not only have a mechanical effect on the
autogenous deformation. For instance, CaO­3% has a clear ’bump’ during the first couple
of hours of hydration, where the sand specimen does not show any sign of this charac­
teristic. Furthermore its autogenous strain is ­1875 µm/m compared to the ­2552 µm/m
for the specimen containing sand.

6.5.2
Skin carbonated CaO
The effect of skin carbonation to retard the expansive reaction of CaO on shrinkage com­
pensation has been examined by dilatometer measurements at 30 °C. The effect of dif­
ferent amounts of CaO added can be seen in Figure 6.17, illustrating the autogenous
deformation of specimens of 13.7% skin carbonated CaO with 2.35%, 2.93% and 5.86%
CaO. The measurements are plotted with the untreated CaO specimens containing 3%
and 6% as well as the reference casting for comparison. The CaO13.7% specimens con­
taining 2.93% and 5.86% CaO were intended to have 3% and 6% CaO, respectively, but
as mentioned previously, the specimens were cast with the skin carbonated samples with­
out consideration of LOI. The true amounts of CaO are therefore lower, and regarding the
comparison of the DOC effect on the autogenous deformation, seen in Figure 6.18, the
44

amounts are not completely identical, but approximately alike.

Figure 6.17: Autogenous deformation of 13.7% skin carbonated specimens with varying amount
of CaO added at 30 °C. For comparison, specimens with 3% and 6% CaO as well as the reference
casting is shown. w/c­ratio = 0.30 for all specimens.
As seen for untreated CaO, the autogenous shrinkage will be compensated proportionally
with increasing amount of skin carbonated CaO, but at a much more substantial degree.
The autogenous deformation seems to develop exponentially with the amount of skin
carbonated CaO added. This is seen for the CaO13.7%­5.86% specimen expanding to
86099 µm/m after only 100 h, which is an expansion of 8.6% of the cement paste. The
other specimens of CaO13.7% also show a significant decrease in shrinkage with a strain
of ­1717 µm/m and ­221 µm/m after 330 h for 2.35% CaO and 2.93% CaO, respectively.
Compared to the other measurements with difference in degrees of carbonation as seen
in Figure 6.18, this compensation is rather small, and not at all in the same extend as for
the 5.86% CaO specimen which has only doubled the amount of CaO added.
Figure 6.18 shows specimens with different degrees of carbonation, that aimed for a 3%
CaO content, but ended in small variations, which may be insignificant. The small dif­
ference still make them comparable, and the specimens are also compared to the 3%
untreated CaO specimen and also CaCO3­3%. The latter proves, that the carbonated
CaO (CaCO3) has a lower influence on shrinkage compensation than that of CaO­3%
with a strain of ­2811 µm/m and 1875 µm/m, respectively. It is seen in the figure, that an
skin carbonating CaO retards the expansive reaction from hydration. This is also seen
more closely in Figure 6.19 illustrating the measurements within the first 100 h. It is seen,
that some skin carbonation is required before a large retarding effect can be noticed.
When observing the deformation of 3.9% skin carbonated CaO, it clearly shows a sig­
nificant hydration reaction of the CaO within the first 20 h reaching ­205 µm/m, but then
the deformation slope shows no sign of expansive reaction. The hydration reaction of
CaO3.9%­2.94% is retarded in regards to untreated CaO, and the same characteristically
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
45
==PAGE:56==
’bump’ can be seen scaled up, yet the induction period seems too short for the speci­
men to reach expansion at an early age compared to specimens with a higher degree of
carbonation.
Regarding the specimens with a higher DOC, they all reach expansion at a certain point,
but the 21.5% skin carbonated specimen is clearly more retarded than the other speci­
mens, first expanding after 545 h. Its full potential cannot be evaluated, since it did not
decrease in expansion before the measurement was stopped. In general the effect of
skin carbonation on the magnitude of expansion is difficult to determine, since the differ­
ent specimens vary a lot and a clear tendency cannot be seen. This is shown for both
the 11.1% and 16.2% skin carbonated specimens’ autogenous deformation shape look­
ing almost identical, while the 13.1% and 13.7% skin carbonated specimens compensate
for the shrinkage at a lower degree. Nonetheless, compared to the untreated CaO spec­
imens, the shrinkage compensation of skin carbonated CaO is abnormal.

The different particle sizes of skin carbonated CaO and their effect on autogenous defor­
mation have been investigated. Figure 6.20 shows the 13.7% skin carbonated specimens
with 2.93% CaO in the particle fractions < 40 µm, 40­90 µm and > 90 µm. The CaO13.7%­
2.93% sample is shown for comparison. The deformation curves clearly show, that the
particle fraction < 40 µm has a low effect on the autogenous deformation compared to the
other particle fractions. After 330 h, this fraction has a strain of ­1522 µm/m, which is very
modest compared to the larger particle fractions with autogenous strains at 37861 µm/m
and 51505 µm/m for 40­90 µm and > 90 µm, respectively. However, the smaller particles
seem to hydrate faster, since the characteristic small ’bump’ has a larger deformation rate
for the smaller particle fraction than the other particle fractions. This is the same obser­
vation in the experiments examining the degree of hydration, which have shown that the
smaller particle sizes have a tendency to hydrate faster.

The particle fractions have been examined further by investigating the subset, that each

2.93% specimen containing all the subsets (100%) of the particle fractions. The smallest
fraction < 40 µm has been found to constitute of the least subset of the particle distribution
being 16%, while the particle fractions 40­90 µm and > 90 µm constitutes of 56% and 28%
respectively. This corresponds to an amount of CaO added of 0.47%, 1.64% and 0.82%
for the particle fractions < 40 µm, 40­90 µm and > 90 µm respectively. There is a lot of
noise shown on the graph of CaO13.7%40­90µm­2.93% and CaO13.7%>90µm­2.93%,
that cannot be explained.
It can be seen, that the middle fraction 40­90 µm constitutes the largest part of the total
shrinkage compensation. The deformation curves of the fractions cannot be superposi­
tioned to achieve the deformation curve from the CaO13.7%­2.93% specimen. Previous
results have shown, that the amount of skin carbonated CaO added have a large effect
on the autogenous deformation. So it is difficult to distinguish the effect of particle size
on the autogenous deformation from the amount of CaO added in the plot seen in Figure
6.21.
