7
Discussion
7.1
Carbonation
7.1.1
Analyses of CaO
For the preliminary analyses of CaO before larger productions of the skin carbonated
samples, a TGA­apparatus has been used. During the examinations, issues occurred.
The first issue observed was that no sign of carbonation of the CaO sample for a CO2
flow rate of 50 mL/min was seen. The immediate thoughts were that either the sample
suffered from ’starvation’ of CO2, meaning that there not enough CO2 is present for the
reaction to take place, or that something is wrong with the apparatus. The former is most
unlikely, since stoichiometric calculations have proved, that there would be excess of CO2
with a flow of 50 mL/min. Nonetheless, the flow rate was increased to 120 mL CO2 per
minute, improving the DOC significantly that supports the ’starvation’ concern. However,
the starvation could be due to the apparatus leaking. This was examined, see Appendix
A.2, and seemed to be a reasonable explanation, yet no errors were established for the
apparatus. Nonetheless, the results still showed unsteady carbonation curves, and some
samples even lost weight. This is especially seen for the DOC curve at 400 °C and 610 °C.
These results are therefore not reliable and only a point of reference for the examinations
made in the tube oven for further production of larger samples.
7.1.2
Production of skin carbonated CaO
The autogenous deformation has been measured for cement pastes containing different
amounts of skin carbonated CaO. The skin carbonated CaO samples have been produced
by heating CaO powder in a tube furnace, with two factors influencing the final degree of
carbonation; time exposed for CO2 and temperature.
Studies have examined the effect of temperature and time on the degree of carbonation.
The mass gain of CaO samples carbonated at different temperatures for different periods
of time has been investigated by Chen et al. [2002]. The study shows, that both the dura­
tion and temperature is of high importance for the carbonation reaction. However, when
the period of time increases, the utilization gets lower, meaning the duration becomes
less important for the degree of carbonation over time.
Hlaing et al. [2016] have examined the process of calcining CaCO3 for carbonating the
calcined CaO again afterwards at different temperatures. It shows, that the sample would
almost fully carbonate within 30 min at high temperatures (700 °C), while lower temper­
atures at 300 °C only skin carbonated the samples within 30 min. However, within this
period, the skin carbonation will happen immediately after CO2 exposure. After the 30
min the carbonation rate will then stagnate over time reaching some kind of equilibrium
for the achieved DOC. This shows, that a higher temperature is needed for full carbona­
tion. Both time exposed for CO2 and temperature have an influence on the carbonation
reaction, and an attempt of illustrating the results obtained in this thesis is shown in Figure
7.1.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
53
==PAGE:64==

Degree of carbonation [%]
Figure 7.1: Illustration of degrees of carbonation as a function of temperature with the correspond­
ing durations of CO2 exposure.
Comparing with the studies made by Chen et al. [2002] and Hlaing et al. [2016], the degree
of carbonation obtained clearly show a tendency of higher DOC for larger temperatures,
and the duration only seem to have an influence when exposed for shorter durations. It
can especially be seen for the 3.9% skin carbonated sample that was achieved by heating
at 300 °C for 2.5 h, with another experiment resulted in a degree of carbonation of 21.5%
when exposed for 518 °C for 1.5 h. This implies, that the CO2 exposure time is not as
crucial for the degree of carbonation as the temperature. This is further supported by the
specimen achieved after the first carbonation experiment. This sample reached a DOC
of 24.0% after 13 h, while another sample reached a DOC of 26.4% after only 3 h. The
only difference was the temperature with an average of 502 °C for the former sample and
518 °C for the latter. This signifies, that the temperature is of much higher significance in
regards to the degree of carbonation compared to the duration when reaching a certain
point.
For a better understanding of the time influence on the carbonation reaction, the degree
of carbonation has been examined over time during the production of the skin carbonated
samples. This is done for one sample exposed for a constant temperature. This exami­
nation is possible with the tube furnace setup, since the inlet of the tube can be opened,
with space for a smaller crucible. The sample can then be taken in and out with the oven
still on. The weight of the sample has been measured after 15 min, 30 min, 60 min and
120 min of CO2 exposure, with cooling under neutral N2­gas before each measurement.
This analysis can be seen in Figure 7.2.
According to the experiment, there is a linear development of the degree of carbonation.
This is probably due to the interruptions after the different durations of carbonation, so it
takes longer time before the DOC has reached its equilibrium. Another reason can be,
that the duration has not lasted long enough before the equilibrium is reached, or that
CaO actually is partially linear proportional with time. However, the larger production of
samples that have not been interrupted show a different tendency, so this is probably not
the case. Another factor can be the sample size, since this sample constituted of 10.68
g, while the samples for larger productions are around 180 g for each crucible. But this
demands further research to be confirmed.
54
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:65==

Figure 7.2: Degree of carbonation over time for a sample heated at 511 °C.
According to Chen et al. [2002], it is possible to produce a sample of same DOC when
exposing a sample at a low temperature for a long duration as at a high temperature
for a short duration. Considering two samples with the same DOC exposed for different
circumstances, it is not known, how the samples will behave when hydrating. It may be
expected, that the skin carbonation effect will be similar, but further investigations must
be made to determine this. In theory, CaO will be produced from calcining CaCO3 and
can be achieved from heating above 900 to 1000 °C. But heating at higher temperatures
is also possible to produce CaO, and depending on the temperature, the CaO produced
will be either soft­burned or hard­burnt. The soft­burned CaO will hydrate more rapidly
than the hard­burnt CaO, and increasing temperature will retard the hydration [Mehta
and Monteiro, 2014]p.164. It may therefore also be expected, that the skin of carbonate
produced may have different characteristics depending on the production circumstances.
7.2
Hydration resistance
The hydration resistance has shown to increase significantly for CaO when treated for
skin carbonation. This is especially seen at low relative humidities, where the samples
did only reach a degree of hydration of 9.00% after 30 days. Eventually, the 13.7% skin
carbonated sample will reach complete hydration after 3 days at relative humidities above
95% and after 7 days above 85%. The internal RH in a normal cement paste system is
above 90% RH during a long time of hydration, so the RH will not decrease to an amount,
where the skin carbonated particles cannot hydrate [Bentz et al., 2000].
From the analyses, it is observed that the skin carbonated samples reach a DOH higher
than 100%, which is not possible stoichiometrically. When surpassing this limit, it raises
doubts, that there are uncertainties associated with the measuring technique. A reason
can be due to the DOC being completely different than measured. However, several DOC
checks have been made, so this is unlikely. Furthermore, the DOH reaches up to 107%,
so it requires a deviation of around 4% in DOC, making the actual DOC being 9.7% (from
the 13.7%), which can most likely only be the case if the calculations are wrong, which is
considered implausible.
Another reason can be the amount of material able to hydrate is larger than specified in
the data sheet for CaO. According to the data sheet, the CaO sample contains 95% CaO
and 1.4% MgO, but there is a standard deviation associated with these mean values,
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
55
==PAGE:66==
so a higher amount present is a likely option. However if this is the case, the untreated
CaO sample will have shown a DOH above 100% as well, while this is not the case.
The actual reason cannot be determined on the spur of the moment, and requires further
examination.
7.3
Autogenous deformation
Experiments with measurement of the autogenous deformation has been performed by
two methods and both at different reaction circumstances. When considering the mea­
surement techniques used, both measures the linear strain of the specimens. The dilatome­
ter is a common method acknowledged in C 1698 [2009], while the tube method used for
manual measurements has not been acknowledged. It is important to emphasize, that the
measuring gauge used for these measurements is credible, but whether the autogenous
conditions are achieved for the specimens during measurement and storage is debatable.
The reaction kinetics and hydration reaction are very dependent on the compounds in
the cement paste and the reaction circumstances. For limiting the different factors that
influence the hydration reactions of the different mixtures, the w/c­ratio is set at 0.30 for all
CaO specimens. Beside the varying amounts of CaO used in the specimens as well as the
respective DOC, that constitute the primary factors influencing the reaction components,
the only other circumstance investigated is the temperature influence. All specimens have
been measured at 30 °C, while some have been chosen for a 20 °C exposure as well.
7.3.1
Measuring techniques
Regarding the dilatometer measurements, the autogenous conditions are almost assured,
meaning the deformation measured is representative of the autogenous deformation of
the specimen. The disadvantage with this measuring technique is, that the transducers
measure with a high accuracy, meaning that the measurements are very susceptible to­
wards changes.
Another disadvantage is the capacity of the corrugated moulds, that will crack when ex­
panded too much. Figure 7.3a shows the CaO13.7%>90µm­2.93% specimen after taking
off the end closures at experiment termination. It is seen, that the mould is completely
cracked, and that the cement paste is crumbling, implying that the required sealing is not
complied with, as one of the criteria for autogenous conditions. The measurements may
therefore become precarious, because the method is not designed for expansions at such
magnitude, leading to a concern that the large deformations measured may not be fully
representative of the actual autogenous deformation. This is also seen for the CaO16.2%­
2.92% specimen with its deformation slope decreasing, but suddenly changes. Adjust­
ment of the specimen in the dilatometer indicate this point from where the deformation
rate changes, and this shows, that the measuring technique may be very vulnerable to
expansions. It is also seen for some specimens experiencing large expansions, that they
show sign of other than linear deformation in the dilatometer, which can be a sign of too
much restraint. This is seen in Figure 7.3b to the outher most right, showing this behaviour
for CaO13.7%>90µm­2.93%.
56
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:67==
(a) CaO13.7%>90µm­2.93% specimen after taking
off the end closures after measurement.
(b) Specimens in dilatomer.
The most right spec­
imen CaO13.7%>90µm­2.93% show signs of other
than linear deformation.
Figure 7.3: Specimens showing concerning behaviour in regards to autogenous deformation mea­
surements.
This concern makes the foundation for the other developed method, where the specimen
is placed in a large tube, sealed in both ends. Since the tube is larger than the specimen, it
is able to deform freely within the tube, only restrained by what is assumed to be little fric­
tion. During storage, the autogenous conditions are almost satisfied as for the dilatomer
method, but issues appear during measurements. The disadvantage is, that the specimen
is taken out of the tube, unsealing the specimen and exposing it to a different atmosphere.
This decreases the RH in the surroundings leading to drying of the specimen, which may
introduce drying shrinkage. However, drying the specimen will first result in loss of free
water, which causes little or no shrinkage, and it first becomes of significance if drying is
continued [Neville, 2011]p.427. This means, that if treating the specimens properly, dry­
ing shrinkage may be efficiently avoided, and the deformation measured represents the
autogenous deformation. Furthermore, it is seen from measurements of the weight after
each deformation measurement, that the weight is almost unchanged. This can be seen
in Table 7.1.
Table 7.1: Weight losses of specimens kept at 20 °C after 41­45 days.

Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
57
==PAGE:68==
If assumed that the specimen does not crumble and lose mass from handling it during
measurements, the specimens lose between 0.26%­0.42% of their weight with the ex­
ception of 2.13% for CaO13.7%­5.86%. The total water added to the mixtures constitutes
of 23­27% of the mass in the cement paste, depending on the amount of additive. After
41­45 days, all the water may not have been chemically bound, so some free water is still
present in the pore structure. This can be investigated by examining the degree of hydra­
tion of the cement paste, with an appropriate model as proposed by Power. However, this
model only includes hydration of cement, and since silica fume and CaO as additive con­
stitute a big part of the mixture, this must be taken into account as well [Munch­Petersen,
2017]. The degree of hydration over time has not been examined for the specimens in
this thesis, and it can therefore not be determined whether the weight change assumed to
be from evaporation of water is considerable in regards to superimposition of other types
of shrinkage to the autogenous deformation measurements.
A more simple way to investigate this concern is to take one sample out of the tube, and
store it in an open atmosphere under surveillance. Once the specimen has lost weight
equal to the weight loss between the two former measuring points, the deformation is
measured. The expectation is, that the specimen will lose weight much faster than seen
between the two former measuring points, and as a result, the specimen will experience
a significant change in shrinkage. If this is not the case, the autogenous conditions are
not satisfied within the tube, and the measurements are therefore invalid in regards to
autogenous deformation.
The assumption of the weight change only due to evaporation of water is not reliable.
Many of the specimens have seemed to crumble, and losing mass from this was unavoid­
able. This is especially seen for CaO13.7%­5.86% and CaO13.7%­2.93%, that can be
disintegrated if wanted to. It can be seen in Figure 7.4 showing a very textured surface.
(a) CaO13.7%­5.86% specimen stored at 20 °C.
(b) CaO13.7%­2.93% specimen stored at 20 °C.
Figure 7.4: Specimens showing concerning cracks in regards to autogenous deformation mea­
surements.
Another issue with the tube method is, that the specimens can first be measured after the
cement paste has reached a certain strength, since it must be in one piece after demould­
ing. This means, that the deformation is probably not plotted from the final setting time as
for the dilatometer measurements, and this may affect the final magnitude measured of
the autogenous deformation. Furthermore, the hydration reaction cannot be followed as
precisely as in the dilatometer due to the intervals between the measurements.
7.3.1.1
Approval of tube method
Despite the concerns regarding the measurements at 20 °C, the results seem reliable.
This is seen when compared to the autogenous deformations at 30 °C in Figure 7.5. The
specimens at 20 °C seem to follow the same development as the specimens at 30 °C
but in a more inhibited development. This is seen for the CaO13.7%­5.86% specimen
that reaches an abnormal expansion at 30 °C, yet the 20 °C specimen follows with almost
58
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:69==
as high expansion. Furthermore, the autogenous shrinkage is lower for the 20 °C spec­
imens, compared to the specimens at 30 °C, which hydration reactions are accelerated
and therefore increases in shrinkage. This implies, that the measurements seem to rep­
resent the autogenous deformation, verifying the measuring technique. It is noticeable,
that the deformation courses at 20 °C are not as smooth as the specimens at 30 °C, but
this is due to the uncertainties with manual measurement as well as the linear fit between
the measuring points.

7.3.2
Reaction circumstances
7.3.2.1
Amount of additive
For all experiments with autogenous deformation containing CaO, it clearly proves that
increasing amount of CaO will increase the shrinkage compensation and eventually lead
to expansion, as expected. This increase in amount is much more effective when the
CaO is skin carbonated, with all specimens reaching expansion within 21 days for mea­
surements containing 2.88%­2.94% CaO. When doubling the amount of 13.7% skin car­
bonated CaO from 2.93% to 5.86% reaches an unexpected expansion magnitude. This
emphasizes, that the autogenous deformation measurements are not linear proportional
with the amount of CaO added and will vary significantly. This is also discussed in Sec­
tion 7.3.3. Despite the uncertainties associated with the measurements mentioned above,
this abnormal difference is not expected, implying that cautiousness must be taken when
dealing with this expansive additive, because it can do more harm than good, introducing
unsoundness, as described in Section 7.7.
7.3.2.2
Temperature influence
The temperature has a huge influence on the hydration kinetics. This is according to
the maturity concept, proposing that the temperature influence on hydration rate is inde­
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
59
==PAGE:70==
pendent on the degree of hydration. It states, that the hydration rate is increased with
temperature increase, and depends on the activation energy of the cementitious material
hydrating. This means, that the temperature will affect the chemical shrinkage and thereby
the autogenous deformation. The maturity concept describes a proportionality between
time and temperature, while this may not be the case for autogenous deformation. Ac­
cording to Jensen and Hansen [1999], the maturity concept cannot be transformed into
the autogenous deformation phenomenon, since it is difficult to distinguish between the
hydration of cement and silica fume in the autogenous deformation courses. When other
additives are present, such as CaO, this makes it even more difficult. This is due to the
difference in activation energy of the reacting materials. Furthermore, as the hydration re­
actions are very dependent on the composition and circumstances, the hydration products
may therefore vary [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014]p.201. So a baseline of the development of
the hydration products cannot be established, and transformation of time is thereby not
applicable to autogenous deformation [Jensen and Hansen, 1999].
Nonetheless, the observations from the autogenous deformation measurements can be
compared and discussed. It is seen, that the magnitude and development is in general
larger and faster for the specimens kept at 30 °C, compared to the specimens at 20 °C,
which are more inhibited. A notable reason is the accelerating hydration reaction from in­
creasing reaction temperature to 30 °C. Regarding the specimens experiencing shrinkage,
this magnitude is in general also larger, and this is probably due to the large shrinkage rate
at start as a results of accelerated hydration, which has not been recorded for the spec­
imens at 20 °C. However, very different from each other, the specimens containing skin
carbonated CaO seem to behave consistently at 20 °C with a tendency of a retardation of
the expansion reaction with increasing DOC.
Compared to the specimens kept at 30 °C, these are much more arbitrary in regards to
induction time before expansion as well as magnitude of the deformation as a function of
DOC. A specimen that especially stands out is the 13.7% skin carbonated CaO­2.93%,
which is the one examined for further investigations, coincidentally. Luckily, this skin car­
bonated sample seems to follow the same tendency as the other skin carbonated sam­
ples, when measured at 20 °C, although it reached higher magnitude of expansion at 20
°C than at 30 °C, which is the only case observed for the skin carbonated specimens. A
reason can be the measuring technique, as described earlier. The uncertainties may be
too big for specimens expanding, which will result in non­consistent measurements. Fu­
ture work is needed for this determination, and a way to examine these concerns can be
to investigate the same sample cast in multiple specimens and follow the measurements
concurrently.
Something worth mentioning is the deformation due to thermal expansion. The thermal
expansion coefficient for concrete is 20 (µm/m)/°C, and with a temperature difference of
10 °C, this will result in a deformation difference of 200 µm/m. Considering small de­
formations, this may be taken into consideration, but the deformations measured in this
thesis reach magnitudes at where this small difference becomes insignificant, why it is
not mentioned in further discussion.
These observations are only based on the measurements within the early age develop­
ment of the cement pastes, and it is therefore yet to be determined, what the long term
development will look like. The specimens with CaO and skin carbonated CaO that have
shown expansive reactions from hydration and a decreasing deformation rate over time at
early ages, are not expected to show another expansion reaction at long­term. Regarding
the specimens containing skin carbonated CaO, which have not shown any sign of expan­
60
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:71==
sive reaction, it may be expected, that an expansion can occur at long­term. However,
the autogenous deformation measurements seem to show a varying tendency, so these
are just assumptions based on the observations made. The actual development has to
be examined within future research.
7.3.3
Shrinkage compensating potential from skin carbonation
For the produced samples of skin carbonated CaO, the compensating effect on autoge­
nous shrinkage has been examined. Considering untreated CaO, the shrinkage com­
pensating effect is considerable, yet a high amount is needed for the specimen to reach
expansion. This is not the case for the skin carbonated specimens, where amounts just
under 3% CaO has shown to reach expansion within 14 days for hydration at 30 °C and 21
days at 20 °C. Containing this amount of CaO, it is observed, that a certain DOC must be
reached before the specimens reach expansion, seen among the 3.9% skin carbonated
CaO specimens at both temperatures. This implies that the hydration reaction appears
too fast and the skin carbonated CaO have almost fully hydrated before having a simi­
lar autogenous shrinkage development as untreated CaO. Song and Kim [1990] mention
that the layer thickness of CaCO3 is proportional to the square root of induction period.
This means, that a certain degree of carbonation must be reached before a significant
increase in induction time can be seen, which supports this observation. For the other
skin carbonated specimens at 30 °C, the induction period before expansion is not consis­
tent. It has some sign of increase in induction time when the DOC increases, but there
are some outliers. This also indicate, that the reaction kinetics and hydration reaction
are very dependent on the circumstances as well as the composition, which may imply
uncertainties with the measuring method. For the specimens kept at 20 °C with the other
measuring method, the induction periods before expansion show a clear tendency for the
DOC. It increases with the DOC, which supports the theory [Song and Kim, 1990].
Regarding the effect of DOC on the magnitude of the autogenous deformation, no clear
tendency has been seen for the measurements at 30 °C, which is another indication of un­
certainties associated with the dilatometer technique for large expansions. Regarding the
specimens kept at 20 °C, the slopes do not indicate what magnitude will be reached yet,
so it cannot be determined if the DOC has an influence. However, the courses follow the
same tendency during the duration of the measurements. Mutual for the measurements
at both temperatures is that some skin carbonation is needed for a significant shrinkage
compensation compared to the untreated CaO. It is expected, that a certain degree of
carbonation will be most optimal in regards to utilization of the amount of CaO added,
but it is difficult to determine this based on the results achieved in this thesis, so further
investigations are required to give an estimation of an optimal DOC.
The untreated CaO prove that deformation is not linear proportional with the amount
added. Regarding the skin carbonated specimens, the deformations prove to reach new
heights by developing exponentially. For the specimens containing 13.7% skin carbon­
ated CaO, varying from 2.35% to 2.93% CaO there is a significant difference. Doubling
the amount of skin carbonated CaO from the latter to the 5.86% CaO specimen turn out to
reach an unimagined expansion. This behaviour of the deformation was never imagined
to reach such magnitude.
This major increase makes the use of skin carbonated CaO uncontrollable as seen for the
CaO13.7%­5.86% specimen advancing to an expansion of 86099 µm/m after only 100 h.
This is an expansion of 8.6% of the cement paste, which is completely unprecedented in
any of the literature investigated in this thesis, that examines autogenous shrinkage com­
pensation by expansive additives. The measurement had to be stopped after the 100
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
61
==PAGE:72==
h since it maxed out the capacity in the dilatometer, why the tube method described in
Section 4.4 was conceived.
To put things in perspective, comparing with the 6% CaO specimen, the CaO has only
been carbonated 13.7%. Of the nearly 6% CaO added, the skin carbonation only con­
stitutes a subset of the CaO added, and relative to all the components in the specimen,
it is only a minimal change of the mixture. Yet, the autogenous deformation reaches
new heights of expansion of 8.6% compared to the CaO­6% specimen with a reduction
of 0.04% after 4 days. This is also the case for several of the specimens with different
degrees of carbonation, compensating for the autogenous deformation at a much larger
degree than for untreated CaO, seen in Figure 6.18. However, this leads to other issues
among unsoundness, which is more thoroughly discussed in Section 7.7.
This signifies, that skin carbonation may be extremely efficient in regards to shrinkage
compensation to utilize the potential of CaO as expansive additive. This is suggested,
because as a result of postponing the hydration reaction, the water has to diffuse through
the skin carbonate to slowly build up a pressure from CaO hydration. Eventually, the skin
carbonate will break when a sufficient hydration pressure is reached, with rapid hydration
following. As a result, the hydration of CaO is ensured to take place after setting, restrain­
ing the reaction products, leading to expansion of the cement body. This further declares
that using skin carbonated CaO for shrinkage compensation may be uncontrollable and
must therefore be treated carefully, because the outcome cannot be predicted. However,
skin carbonation proposes whole new possibilities within shrinkage compensation, and it
is of the author’s belief, that this is is still very unexplored and much further research can
be done within this area.
It is known, that some cement remain unhydrated in mixtures with low w/c­ratio due to
excess of cement [Neville, 2011]p.13. A concern is therefore, that the almost inert skin
carbonation will result in a complete inhibition of the hydration of CaO, meaning the full
potential of CaO may not be taken advantage of. However, this concern is clearly refuted
when observing the enormous expansions for the skin carbonated samples, showing that
the potential of expanding the cement paste is much higher for skin carbonated CaO
than untreated CaO. Even the specimens not showing any sign of expansion are seen
to compensate for the autogenous shrinkage compared to the reference specimen not
containing any CaO. This is at least the observation made for the degrees of carbonation
examined, but a DOC may be reached at some point, where a negative effect in regards
to shrinkage compensation is introduced, compared to untreated CaO, yet this is for future
examinations.
7.4
Particle size
This section will discuss the effect of particle size on hydration of CaO. The particle size
distribution of the 13.7% skin carbonated CaO has been investigated by manual sieving
analysis. The analysis has shown sieving residuals of 28% and 56% for the fractions >
90 µm and 40­90 µm, respectively. This means, that the fraction < 40 µm constitutes of a
16% subset of the entire particle distribution. The sample size used for the sieving analysis
constituted of 10.13 g, so the obtained results can be received with scepticism. A concern
with the particle size distribution is that the particles may agglomerate and form a larger
particle since the particles are very small. This can lead to the particles agglomerating or
even being residual by the sieving threads attracting the particles by electrostatic forces,
and the particle size distribution achieved may therefore not be representative.
62
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:73==
7.4.1
Influence on autogenous deformation
The influence of particle size has been examined in regards to autogenous deformation.
Regarding the untreated CaO, it shows that larger particles will reduce the shrinkage more
than smaller particles, as expected. This is due to the higher specific surface for smaller
particles than for larger particles. This means, that the smaller particles will hydrate faster
and are therefore more likely to have reacted before setting, limiting the expansion. Even
though, the particle fraction above 90 µm constitutes of a very small part of the CaO, this
subset may have a huge influence. When observing the autogenous deformations, the
particle fractions < 40 µm and 40­90 µm containing 3% CaO compensate less than the
CaO­3% specimen, meaning that the CaO contain particles above 90 µm with a large
significance. As mentioned earlier, there are uncertainties associated with the measure­
ments, but a bigger ’bump’ after around 8 h is seen for the CaO­3% specimen, and this
bump only decreases in magnitude when the particle size fraction decreases. This in­
dicates, that the size of the particles matter in regards to the expansive reaction from
hydration, and that larger particles are present in the CaO sample.
This difference in particle size has a much larger effect when skin carbonated, seen for
the autogenous deformations for the different fractions of the 13.7% skin carbonated CaO.
The smallest particle fraction < 40 µm does not show any sign of expansion, while the frac­
tions between 40 and 90 µm as well as > 90 µm show a large deformation, only containing
2.93% CaO. These large magnitudes indicate, that the larger fractions are the ones con­
tributing to the significant shrinkage compensation. For untreated CaO, the significant
factor is the hydration resistance of the particles. The small particles hydrate much faster
than the larger particles, which can be of high significance whether the hydration of CaO
happens in the early dormant period or just before setting. This is also the observations
made in other studies of the particle size influence [Polat et al., 2015].
When skin carbonated, this resistance is increased drastically, so the issue with CaO hy­
drating too fast is solved. This abnormal difference in autogenous deformation is therefore
not necessarily dependent on the hydration rate anymore. It is of the authors belief, that
the particle size influence is due to the small particles being able to expand within the
fine pores. The larger particles do not fit within the small pores and are therefore more
restrained, leading to a larger deformation of the system when the CaO hydrates.
This has made ground for further analyses of the particle size. Each particle fraction has
been examined for the contribution to the shrinkage compensation of the entire 13.7%
skin carbonated CaO sample by its subset that constitutes of the entire particle size dis­
tribution. These measurements has not shown any significant shrinkage compensation
both for the specimens at 30 °C and 20 °C. This is probably due to the low amounts of
CaO added of 0.47%, 1.64% and 0.82% for the fractions < 40 μm, 40­90 μm and > 90
μm respectively. At first glance, it seems, that the 40­90 µm particle fraction will reduce
the shrinkage the most out of all fractions compared to > 90 µm and the smallest fraction
< 40 µm. This is definitely due to the difference in amount of CaO added, since previous
results have shown, that the deformation is very susceptible towards the amount of skin
carbonated CaO added.
7.4.2
Influence on hydration of skin carbonated CaO
The hydration resistance has been investigated for the different particle fractions of 13.7%
skin carbonated CaO. In general, the smallest fraction < 40 µm hydrates the fastest and
the largest fraction > 90 µm is more retarded. This is expected, since the fineness increase
with smaller particles, and it is also observed for the autogenous deformation measure­
ments. This is also seen when compared in Figure 7.6, showing the DOH at different
relative humidities for an exposure of 2 days. It is seen, that there is a clear tendency for
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
63
==PAGE:74==
faster hydration with decreasing particle size. This difference can be seen varying up to
24.25% at the RH = 85% between the < 40 µm fraction and the > 90 µm fraction. The
smallest fraction has seemed to almost fully hydrate as for CaO compared to the larger
fractions at the highest RH of 98%.

7.4.3
Influence on carbonation
The particle fractions of 13.7% skin carbonated CaO has shown a difference in hydration
rate both seen from the autogenous deformation measurements and hydration resistance
examinations. It is then interesting to examine, if it is a result of the particle fractions having
different degrees of carbonation. This is done by checking the DOC of each fraction,
by heating in an oven to 1050 °C, see Appendix B.3 for method. It is the expectation,
that larger particles may not have carbonated as much as smaller particles, due to the
difference in specific surface area. The DOC of the particle fractions can be seen in Table
7.2.

The degree of carbonation seems to differ in the particle fractions, but the assumption of
a higher DOC of smaller particles are not completely right, if relying on the examinations
made. The results show, that the fraction 40­90 µm has the lowest DOC in both tests,
while the fraction > 90 µm has the highest DOC in the second test. There is no logical
consistency in the DOC measured, implying that it may vary significantly depending on
the bit of sample taken at each measurement, because it is just a fraction of the entire
sample, risking that the measurement becomes uncertain. This is especially seen for
the second test showing all fractions with a larger DOC than the sample constituting of
all the fractions, which is not possible. It is therefore difficult to evaluate which DOC is
representative of each fraction. Nonetheless, observing the autogenous deformation of
the different particle fractions in Figure 6.20, the degrees of carbonation of the different

particle fractions are not considered to differ at such a degree, that it affects the expansive
reactions this much.
The results of the degrees of carbonation achieved from every sample also prove, that
time has an insignificant influence on the DOC compared to the influence of temperature.
Although the small particles are assumed to skin carbonate faster than larger particles,
the duration of the experiments have lasted long enough for every fraction to reach the
corresponding DOC. This further indicate that a very small variation in DOC between the
particle fractions are expected, which is also seen for the DOC checks in Table 7.2.
7.5
SEM
The particles have been investigated further by scanning electron microscope (SEM). Us­
ing SEM, it is possible to inspect the particles at a very close distance to see the shape
and surface, and hopefully, an estimation of the form after carbonation can be given. The
samples examined are untreated CaO, CaCO3 and Ca(OH)2 for first to identify charac­
teristics of the three conditions, that the 10.2% and 21.5% skin carbonated samples may
include. It has to be emphasized, that the sample size is very small in a SEM analysis,
meaning that only small particles are represented in the analysis. The sizes shown are
therefore not representative of the entire particle size distribution in the samples.
Figure 7.8, 7.9 and 7.10 show an image of CaO, CaCO3 and Ca(OH)2, respectively. It
can be seen, that the particles, both shape and surface, differ from each other. Without
defining the actual shapes, the untreated CaO grains seem more cubical and spherical,
with a relatively smooth surface. This is very different from the CaCO3 sample showing
needle like crystals, with what seems like a more textured surface. The Ca(OH)2 sample
also shows signs of a more textured surface, but it looks more like CaO. However, it can be
sensed, that the crystals are more hexagonal shaped compared to the CaO. It is difficult
to determine accurately, but mutual for all the images, there is an indication of very small
particles agglomerating, and assembling into larger particles. This is mostly seen for the
CaO particles.

Having some of these characteristics in mind, the 10.2% and 21.5% skin carbonated

same type of particles found in the untreated CaO sample are seen in this image, however
the particles seem to have grown more together. This may be a result of skin carbona­
tion. When inspecting the 10.2% skin carbonated CaO, seen in Figure 7.12 and 7.13, the
surface seem to have a very textured surface compared to the 21.5% skin carbonated
sample, which resembles less textured surface, yet not smooth. This is especially seen
in the latter figure. This may also be due to the zoom of the image, making it more difficult
to identify surface characteristics. In both images, it also seems, that some kind of bond
has developed between the particles, as for the CaO21.5% sample. A study by Chen
et al. [2002] show a cross­section of a skin carbonated specimen examined by SEM. The
SEM image shows that there is a characteristic thickness of the CaCO3 forming the skin
carbonation, and that this is rather textured compared to the CaO. So the observations by
SEM may indicate, that the skin carbonation treatment can make the particle grains grow
together and will make the surface more textured.

7.6
Time zero problematic
In this section, a definition of the final setting time is attempted on the basis of the exper­
iments made in this thesis. The final setting time, also known as the time zero, T0, is the
time between water addition and where the rigid skeleton formed can support the tensile
stresses, indicating the transition from suspension state to solid state. This is also called
the percolation threshold, since it is the time up until the hydration products have formed
a percolated structure throughout the system [Cunha et al., 2016].
The time zero problematic in regards to autogenous deformation is widely known within
cement research, because the setting time is influenced by several factors, and is very
dependent on the hydration reaction. This includes the composition of cement clinkers
and cementitious additives as well as the reaction circumstances [Neville, 2011]p.19. If
the time zero is not determined correctly, the autogenous deformation may be estimated
inaccurately [Darquennes et al., 2011].
7.6.1
Deformation measurement
The final setting time is important to determine for the measurements in the dilatometer,
since the deformation is measured immediately after casting. The reason for the au­
togenous deformations are not plotted from this very moment is because the start has a
floating point zero due to its plastic, fluid state. Even though this phase may still represent
the apparent volume change, or the autogenous deformation, this state is uninteresting in
regards to investigating the autogenous deformation, because a solid cement paste has
not developed yet, meaning that cracks cannot propagate which constitute the problem
with autogenous shrinkage [Jensen and Hansen, 2001].
70
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:81==
An acknowledged method to determine the initial and final setting time is by Vicat needle
which is approved by DS/EN 196­3 [2016]. This is a mechanical test that indicates the
point of time, from where the paste becomes unworkable and begins to develop strength,
respectively. It is of the author’s belief, that the final setting time with the latter characteris­
tic is from where all the autogenous deformation measurements are shown in this thesis.
However, the final setting times determined are not by Vicat test, but from analyses of the
deformation curves. This is due to the dilatometer measurements taking place at 30 °C,
while the Vicat test follows a standard procedure under a temperature at 20 ± 1 °C. The
definition of the final setting time by analyzing the deformation is based on the mechanical
development of the paste from hydration, since deformation is a mechanical behaviour. It
may therefore be expected, that the final setting times determined by this method can be
comparable with measurements by the Vicat test. However, since the deformation rate
can only be analyzed for the specimens kept at 30 °C, an equal comparison cannot be
made. Whether the Vicat test will show reliable results at 30 °C is not investigated.

Figure 7.14: Autogenous deformation (green) and deformation rate (yellow) of Ref­0.30 as a func­
tion of time from water addition. The numbers indicate the three points, that recur for the defor­
mation rate for all deformation measurements.
The analyses of the deformation curves used in this thesis consist of evaluating the de­
formation rates, which are found by numerical differentiation of the deformation curves.
The courses all show three characteristic points that all seem to recur for the deforma­
tion rates, indicated in Figure 7.14. For most of the specimens, the deformation rate is a
shrinkage rate, but some specimens experience a positive deformation rate, which implies
expansion.
As seen in the figure, the deformation rate is very erratic at start before turning more
smooth and stable. The erratic phase is assumed to be due to adjustment of the specimen
in the dilatometer as well as deformation during the the dormant period. The deformation
rate during the dormant period up until the more stable part is therefore not considered to
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
71
==PAGE:82==
be part of the setting time analysis.
The first characteristic point indicates a time from where the shrinkage rate increases. The
second point is the largest shrinkage rate reached when ignoring the erratic phase, and
the third point is from where the deformation rate has decreased and become more steady.
These points vary depending on the specimen, primarily the first and third point, just before
increase in shrinkage rate and after it has been steady, respectively. These points are not
as easily determined as where the shrinkage rate is largest. So the use of fitting methods
is required to find the point that represents the first and third characteristic at best. It
is important to emphasize, that the actual time for these points are not determined in
the figure, but are mainly illustrated for the understanding of the deformation rate of the
specimens.
Two other deformation rate courses can be seen in Figure 7.15, proving the significant
difference in the courses of the deformation rate by using CaO and MgO as additive. It
is seen, that the periods up to the maximum shrinkage rates ignoring the erratic phases,
are very different, and that the indication of the first characteristic is difficult to point out,
especially for the MgO8% specimen in Figure 7.15b.

The hypothesis is, that the increase in shrinkage rate between point 1 and 2 happens from
unrestrained chemical shrinkage as a result of hydration due to the paste being soft. Dur­
ing this period, the paste will become more stiff, until it has developed a skeleton of rigid
solid. The second point from where the shrinkage rate experiences a significant decrease
until point 3 is then an indication of the further development of rigid solid in the paste, mak­
ing it capable of resisting the volume change from chemical shrinkage at a larger degree.
The second point where the shrinkage rate is largest before reaching a steady phase
has been chosen as the indication of the final setting time, and the deformation follow­
ing this point is then considered the autogenous deformation. This interpretation is also
supported by the measurements of the specimens containing 6% and 9% CaO showing
expansion very early on, seen in Figure 7.16. Expansion is only possible, if there is a
skeleton of rigid solid strong enough to restrain the expansive reaction. Even though, the
specimens experience a rather quick expansion, both measurements have an indication
of a maximum shrinkage rate before expanding, implying that a shrinkage from hydration
must happen before a possible expansion can occur. This supports the hypothesis, that
an increase in shrinkage rate is a result of creation of hydration products, and that the
maximum shrinkage rate can be an indication of final setting time, where the hydration
products have created a rigid solid.

There are uncertainties associated with the method chosen, since the large shrinkage
rate before becoming steady may span across a duration of time and not indicate a clear
maximum shrinkage rate at a certain point of time. This can both be seen in Figure 7.14 as
well as Figure 7.15 showing that the maximum shrinkage rate may stretch over a duration
of time, which for the MgO­8% specimen is around 30 min. Since the final setting time
point is at the largest shrinkage rate, plotting from a different time can be of high impor­
tance in regards to the magnitude of the autogenous deformation following this point. For
MgO­8%, the autogenous deformation can vary up to 426 µm/m if plotting in the beginning
or end of the 30 min period. This is probably the reason why the MgO­specimens are not
consistent in regards to autogenous shrinkage within ∼14 days. For the Ref­0.30 speci­
men, this time is around 25 min and can introduce a deviation in autogenous deformation
of 498 µm/m. However, this is primarily an issue for very early age deformation, since the
specimens is expected to evolve to be consistent after longer periods, according to the
slopes of the MgO­specimens in Figure 5.4.
If considering either point 1 or 3 as the final setting time, this period of time may vary even
more due to the large variation in deformation rate around these points, which may intro­
duce even bigger differences in magnitude. The autogenous deformation measurements
propose a relative consistency in regards to composition of cement pastes when ignoring
the few exceptions with MgO, which is another reason for the largest shrinkage rate to in­
dicate a reliable final setting time. Furthermore, the skin carbonated specimens deform at
such a degree, that the concerning deviation in shrinkage during the period of the largest
shrinkage rate may be insignificant. The lack of consistency experienced in the skin car­
bonated deformation measurements is thereby not due to this uncertainty. This concern
may be relevant for some measurements, but in regards to the primary investigations
made in this thesis, this source of error is considered insignificant.
7.6.2
Temperature observation
Several studies have been made to determine the final setting time in other ways. One
repeated technique is to observe the development of the temperature during hydration. To
follow the temperature development, a sensor has been cast into a specimen placed in the
dilatometer for simulating the conditions for the specimens at 30 °C. Following the temper­
ature development, the final setting time is determined chemically, since the temperature
course is a result of the hydration process. Observing the temperature development, the
temperature is expected to increase when hydrating, because the hydration process is an
exothermic reaction [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014]p.209.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
73
==PAGE:84==
Figure 7.17 shows the temperature development of CaO­9% and CaO21.5%­2.88% plot­
ted with the corresponding deformation curve.
It is seen, that a significant tempera­
ture increase occur due to the exothermic hydration reaction. The consideration is then,
which part of the temperature development indicates the final setting time. According to
Shahroodi et al. [2020], the final setting time is somewhere between the start and end of
the inclined temperature development, leaning towards the start of this heating process.

An estimation of the point, from where the temperature starts to increase rapidly has
been made. The method used for this, divided the data set into a horizontal part and an
inclined part. These data sets can then be fitted by linear regression, and the interception
point, where the slope will change significantly, has been chosen as the final setting time
indication. This has been done in Matlab using the k­means clustering method, which
has divided the data set into two parts, that result in the best fit for linear regression.
Figure 7.18 shows examples of this method in use on the CaO­9% and CaO21.5%­2.88%
specimen. The rest of the fittings can be seen in Appendix C.6, and the Matlab script can
be seen in Appendix C.6.1.

It is seen, that the setting times vary from specimen to specimen, meaning that the ad­

Figure 7.19: Plot of setting times.
The influence of degree of carbonation on the final setting time has been shown in Figure
7.19b. The specimens shown are the ones of almost same CaO content, with the small
difference considered insignificant. It is clear to see, that the degree of carbonation does
not seem to have any considerable effect on the final setting time. However, it is rather
retarded when the CaO is skin carbonated, since the 3.9% skin carbonated CaO sample
containing 2.94% CaO having a final setting time of 6.5 h compared to the 5.3 h for the
3% untreated CaO specimen.
The setting time depending on the particle fraction is investigated and can be seen in
Figure 7.20. The untreated CaO show to have a faster setting time for the smaller particle
fraction < 40 µm than the fraction 40­90 µm. A reasonable explanation is, that the smaller
particles will hydrate faster due to the larger specific surface. It is seen, that the skin
carbonated fraction are retarded compared to the untreated CaO fraction. However it
is also seen, that the amount of skin carbonated CaO added has no impact on the final
setting time, with the particle fractions consisting of the subsets actually having a shorter
final setting time than the specimens with higher amount. This supports the hypothesis,
that the amount of CaO added have an insignificant, if any, effect on the setting time when
skin carbonated.

Regarding the specimens containing 10% sand but grinded for different durations, it is

expected for an inert material, but the size variation clearly prove to affect the setting time.
7.6.4
Comparison of methods
Regarding the measured final setting times, a comparison has been made for values
determined by the deformation rate and the temperature development, seen in Figure
7.21. When comparing the setting times determined by both methods, it may be expected,
that the time from temperature measurement may be earlier than the time determined by
deformation rate analysis. This is due to the temperature development being an indication
of the hydration reaction, and before any hydration products are formed, heat has to be
produced. And because the final setting time from deformation measurements are set
after a formation of hydration products, a difference in setting time is expected.
The figure clearly shows, how the deformation measurements have consistently set later
than the temperature measurements with the exception of CaO­9%. This implies, that
either the time from deformation measurements are set too late or the time from tempera­
ture development are set too early. The latter is most likely to be the case, since the time
is taken right before the temperature starts to increase. According to the theory, hydration
is required before the skeleton of rigid solid is formed, and since the hydration reaction
produces heat, the time chosen by this method is probably set too early.

the setting time from temperature development must be defined later on the temperature
graph. Shahroodi et al. [2020] also state that the final setting time lies somewhere during
the inclined temperature increase. This is supported by the measurements performed in
this thesis.

Figure 7.22: Comparison of the time zero, T0, determined from deformation rate (yellow) and

If the setting times determined from the deformation rates in Figure 7.22 are to be indicated
on the associated temperature graphs, the time zero will correspond to a point around
midways during the temperature increase in both cases. When comparing with the theory,
the sudden increase may indicate a significant increase in hydration reaction, and the
temperature peak may imply that the majority of the compounds have hydrated and a
certain degree of hydration has been reached for the paste. A point chosen as the final
setting time around the middle of this temperature increase may therefore be assumed
to be reliable. Since these areas correspond with the setting times determined by the
deformation measurements, the maximum shrinkage rate before steadying may therefore
be an honest indication of the final setting time.
7.7
Perspectivation
7.7.1
Unsoundness
CaO has been examined as shrinkage compensating additive in regards to autogenous
deformation. It has been examined both as untreated and skin carbonated, to evaluate the
potential for compensating the autogenous shrinkage. From the experiments, CaO has
proved to be a significant shrinkage reducing additive, with the skin carbonation strength­
ening this ability leading to extreme expansions. However, concerns with using expansive
additives such as CaO or MgO have been reported to cause the known phenomenon un­
soundness [Neville, 2011]p.51.
Unsoundness is described as a considerable expansion, which can result in a disruption
of the hardened cement paste. This is thereby a large problem after the cement paste
begins to develop strength. Expansion of such degree may be due to a retarded hydration
or other reaction of compounds such as CaO or MgO [Neville, 2011]p.51. Unsoundness
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
79
==PAGE:90==
can lead to cracking and pop­outs among other things, which harms the structure ex­
posed for this phenomenon [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014]p.148. This issue goes back many
years and was first recognized in the 1880s, where a number of concrete bridges and
buildings in France and Germany reported failure and had to be rebuilt. This was due to
the cements containing up to 30% MgO, which introduced cracks from expansion within
2 years. This led to restrictions of the MgO content permissible in cement [Mehta and
Monteiro, 2014]p.163. The MgO specimens examined in this thesis did not show any sig­
nificant sign of expansion within 14 days, but it is unknown whether there would have
been a potential problem if the measurements were continued. The following research
has then found, that free CaO have the same deleterious effect. However, this is very
dependent on the burning temperature during production, with soft­burned CaO hydrat­
ing more rapidly than hard­burnt CaO [Mehta and Monteiro, 2014]p.164. The CaO used
in this thesis is lightly burned between 1100­1200 °C, so hydration will happen rapidly,
and unsoundness at long­term may therefore not be expected. Nonetheless, the large
expansions observed for the specimens containing skin carbonated CaO may therefore
be a huge concern regarding unsoundness. Indication of this issue can also be seen in
Figure 7.4, showing pop­outs and cracks due to expansion for the 13.7% skin carbonated
CaO specimens kept at 20 °C with 2.93% CaO and 5.86% CaO. The white spots is an
indication of pop­outs.
In theory, the use of CaO as expansive additive will enhance the strength development
at early ages as a result of faster setting [Seo et al., 2020]. This is also seen for the CaO
specimens reducing the time zero with increasing amounts. This may seem as a benefit
at first glance, but according to Seo et al. [2020], the strength will not reach as high a
strength as for specimens without the expansive additive. This is another matter that has
to be considered before using CaO in larger amounts. The strength development is not
looked into in this thesis, so it cannot be verified, whether this is the case, but further
research is expected to clarify this.
7.7.2
Alkali silica reaction (ASR)
Another expansive reaction associated with unsoundness is the alkali silica reaction. This
is a phenomenon, where the alkalis in the cement paste constituting of Na2O and K2O will
form an alkali­silicate gel by reacting with the siliceous aggregates. The alkali­silicate gel
is formed on the aggregates, either in the pores or on the surface. This gel imbibes with
water and this reaction increases in volume. Because of restraint of the volume increase
expansion may occur, as for CaO hydration [Neville, 2011]p.144.
Opposite of CaO hydration, the ASR happens very slowly. However, the reaction rate is
determined by the aggregate size, with smaller aggregates reacting at first, while larger
takes longer time before reacting [Multon et al., 2010; Zhang et al., 1999]. This is the
same tendency as seen for CaO hydration in regards to particle sizes. For the litera­
ture examined, whether the larger particles will expand more than the smaller particles is
very dependable on the reactive aggregate. Nonetheless, expansions up to 1.2% during
extreme conditions has been measured and this is stated to cause problems. With up
to 8.6% expansion observed in cement pastes with skin carbonated CaO, it implies that
cautiousness must be taken in regards to unsoundness.
80
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:91==
8
Conclusion
This thesis has examined the effect of skin carbonation on hydration of CaO. This include
the ability of CaO to compensate for autogenous shrinkage in cement paste, and the
effect of skin carbonation as a retarding agent for the hydration reaction with the intention
of utilizing the potential of CaO for compensating for the autogenous shrinkage.
The primary investigations have constituted of measuring the autogenous deformation
of cement pastes containing varying amounts of untreated­ and skin carbonated CaO
with a variation of degrees of carbonation at both 20 °C and 30 °C. Furthermore, the
hydration resistance of CaO has been examined including the effect of skin carbonation
treatment to retard the hydration reaction. Examinations of the particles of both CaO and
skin carbonated CaO have also been conducted.
From the experiments it can be concluded that:
• Skin carbonation of CaO is possible, and it is dependent on time and temperature
circumstances.
• Skin carbonation of CaO proves to increase the hydration resistance of CaO, re­
tarding its hydration reaction significantly.
• Autogenous shrinkage of cement paste is reduced with amount of CaO added. Fur­
thermore, the autogenous deformation is accelerated with increase in temperature.
• Cement pastes containing untreated CaO cannot reach expansion unless large
amounts are added. Cement pastes containing skin carbonated CaO can reach
extreme expansions for much smaller amounts; Some show expansion up to 8.6%
after 4 days compared to a reduction of 0.04% observed for a cement paste with
similar amount of CaO, but without the skin carbonation treatment.
• For both untreated­ and skin carbonated CaO, smaller particles hydrate faster than
larger particles. Furthermore, increase in particle size prove to increase the com­
pensation for autogenous deformation.
From this, it can be concluded that skin carbonation of CaO propose a remarkable poten­
tial for a more efficient compensation of the autogenous shrinkage.

Appendices
==PAGE:98==
A
Methods
A.1
Casting
A.1.1
Purpose
The cement paste castings are the foundation for the autogenous deformation experi­
ments. The casting process follows DS/EN 196­1:2016, but a more thorough procedure
has been stated beneath.
A.1.2
Procedure
• Find the necessary items for mixing: Bowl, blade, mixer, timer, spoon, moulds and
materials.
• Weigh off the cement, silica fume and superplasticizer in a mixing bowl for the de­
sired casting. Expansive additives such as MgO or CaO must be weighed off in a
separate enclosed container to prevent it from hydrating. Water is also weighed off
in a different container.
• Prepare the respective moulds for the casting to be placed in. For autogenous
deformation experiments, a corrugating mould is used. See figure 4.1c
• When ready to mix, place the bowl in a mixer and stir the powder to a uniform mass.
Just before water addition, add the expansive additive and stir it briefly. Add water
at last and start the timer.
• Mix the cement paste continuously for around 5 min ± 30 sec depending on the
rheology of the paste. During mixing, make sure to use a spoon to scrape the
edges once in a while to assure the paste adhering to the bowl’s wall and bottom is
included appropriately in the mix.
• When mixing is done, pour the cement paste into the respective moulds and enclose
them when they are filled up.
88
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:99==
A.2
Thermogravimetric Analysis (TGA)
A.2.1
Procedure
• Make sure, that the gas(es) needed for the experiment has been connected properly.
• Input the needed information in the program from Netzsch.
• Edit the settings for the temperature step(s) and gas flow for each phase.
• When the experiment is set accordingly, get ready to place your specimen in the
TGA.
• Find a clean crucible for the material that has to be examined.
• Follow the instructions for automatic taring to place the crucible with the specimen.
Note the weight of the specimen yourself just in case.
• When ready, run the experiment.
A.2.2
Concerns
Several concerns about the TGA results occurred during the course with the apparatus.
The first concern was, that there might be a leakage of the system, since the material
did not react with the reactive CO2 atmosphere. This was tested by a CO2 measuring
device, showing a significant increase in ppm CO2 around the apparatus compared to the
hallway; Around 750 ppm CO2 and 550 ppm CO2, respectively. Luckily, this is not enough
to worry about regarding health issues.
Other experiments were performed with connecting the outlet atmosphere to a rubber
hose leading to a glass of water. This should show bubbles indicating a natural outlet of
the atmosphere. However, only a weak sign of bubbles occurred, again indicating, that
the system might leak in transit through the TGA.
In general, these experiments emphasized, that the results might be untrustworthy for
carbonation experiments where the sample should react with the atmosphere, why initial
tests of carbonating larger samples in the tube furnace were run.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
89
==PAGE:100==
A.3
Carbonation by tube furnace
A.3.1
Purpose
The purpose with the tube furnace is to carbonate larger CaO samples for further exper­
iments such as autogenous deformation measurements as well as investigation of the
hydration resistance.
A.3.2
Procedure
• Put CaO in the two Al2O3 crucibles and note the start weight. Transport the crucibles
containing the CaO in a box with a lid, so the CaO is exposed as little as possible to
the atmosphere and will not make a mess if dropped on the way to the tube furnace.
• Place the two crucibles in the tube, and slowly push them inwards with the inlet
mount, until the inlet mount is at place for attachment. Immediately turn on the N2
gas at 2.5 NL/min for a non­reactive environment.
• Turn on the oven and heat to the desired temperature. The samples are placed
between two electrodes measuring the temperature, so the wanted temperature
must be around the mean of the two temperatures measured.
• Shift to CO2 gas at 2.5 NL/min when the temperature has reached equilibrium around
the desired temperature and run the experiment for a chosen period of time.
• Turn off the oven and change back to N2 gas once the time has come and wait for
the samples to cool down.
• When the samples have cooled down, note the weight for calculation of the degree
of carbonation, and immediately put the skin carbonated samples in containers for
further storage.
90
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:101==
A.4
RH­exposure
A.4.1
Purpose
The purpose with this experiment is to analyse the hydration resistance of CaO and 13.7%
skin carbonated CaO. The 13.7% skin carbonated CaO has also been divided into the
following 3 fractions, also examined for hydration resistance; < 40 µm, 40 < x < 90 µm
and > 90 µm. The samples are exposed for 7 different relative humidities: 23%, 43%,
65%, 75%, 85%, 95% and 98%, from saturated salt solutions of CH3COOK, KCO3·H2O,
NaNO2, NaCl, KCl, KNO3 and K2SO4, respectively. The samples are kept at these hu­
midities for 1, 2, 3, 7, 14 and 30 days.
A.4.2
Procedure
• Gather enough small glasses for each sample and name them all.
• Weigh off each sample for each exposure time and RH and note the weight, both of
the glass and sample. Take one RH at a time.
• Carefully place the samples in the desiccator and evacuate it.
• Repeat for every RH and note the time.
• When samples have to be taken out after each exposure interval, carefully put air
into the desiccators by holding your thumb in front of the air hole.
• Take the samples out, that has to be measured, and evacuate the desiccator again.
• Weigh the samples, and note the weight. Now put them in the oven at 105 °C for 60
min, to assure, that eventual free water has evaporated. This time has been chosen,
since examinations showed, that heating for 30 min or 2 h did not affect the degree
of hydration for a specimen already hydrated. This is also proved in Appendix B.2.
• After being in the oven for 60 min, take the sample out, and weigh the samples again
when cooled down, and note the weight.
• The degree of hydration can then be found.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
91
==PAGE:102==
A.5
Ball Mill grinding
A.5.1
Purpose
The ball mill is used to crush the aggregates into smaller particles. The idea is, that the
particles will end up having a similar particle size distribution or specific surface area as
both MgO and CaO. This is for comparison of both the effect of particle sizes on autoge­
nous deformation as well as comparing the mechanical characteristics, since the aggre­
gates are inert and will exclusively have a mechanical influence on the shrinkage. In this
experiment, the sand was crushed for 48h, but samples was taken out after 6h, 12h and
24h.
A.5.2
Procedure
• Fill a ball mill with marble balls as in figure 4.9 to around 30% capacity, with a wide
selection of ball sizes ­ half of them small, half of them medium/big.
• Add the aggregates, that has to be crushed, up to the edge of the balls. A ball mill
this size has a capacity of around 1­1.5 kg of aggregates.
• Before grinding, make sure, that the ball mill is secured on the spinner.
• Start grinding for the desired period of time.
92
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:103==
A.6
Sieving
A.6.1
Purpose
Sieving is needed to divide both the pure CaO­ and skin carbonated CaO particles into
different fractions. The fractions looked into are x < 40 µm, 40 µm < x < 90 µm and x >
90 µm. A concern could be if the CaO would hydrate when sieved, because it is exposed
to atmospheric air. However according to Appendix B.2, it is proven, that this is of little
concern regarding the pure CaO, while of no concern at all with skin carbonated CaO.
A.6.2
Procedure
• Gather the 40 µm and 90 µm sieves and the material to be sieved.
• Place the sieves on top of each other with increasing fraction upwards. Remember
to have a solid bottom where the smallest fraction can be detained.
• Sieve the material through by brushing it through the sieves with a brush. This is
done for every fraction, starting from the largest.
• Lastly, place the sieved fractions into sealed containers for storage.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
93
==PAGE:104==
B
Experiments
B.1
Loss on ignition
B.1.1
Purpose
Loss on ignition is the mass lost during heating at high temperatures usually consisting
of hydrates or carbonates. This experiment examines the LOI of the materials used.
Furthermore, a model for LOI as a function of temperature is proposed.
B.1.2
Procedure
• Weigh off a sample of the material desired to check for loss on ignition. Note the
sample weight.
• Place the sample in the oven at 1050°C for around 30 min assuring the whole spec­
imen has been heated up.
• Gently take the sample out of the oven while carefully placing it in a desiccator
ensuring a low risk of atmosphere exposure.
• After cooling, weigh the sample again, and the loss on ignition can be found.
B.1.3
Results
The material investigated for loss on ignition is CaO. The results can be seen in Table
B.1.

CaO into account when exposed for different temperatures during carbonation.

==PAGE:105==
B.2
Hydration resistance
B.2.1
Purpose
The purpose with this experiment is to check for the hydration resistance of the materi­
als under different conditions. The conditions looked into is storage in an open container
at room temperature and when heating specimens at 105°C. The incentive for checking
hydration over time when heating CaO came from a sudden weight gain during loss on
ignition experiments. Also, checking for hydration over time at room temperature is inter­
esting, since the specimens are exposed to the atmosphere for a duration up to 30 min in
several experiments. The weight gain during the experiment is definitely from hydration,
since results from carbonation experiments show, that temperature have a high influence
on the carbonation process, see Section 6.1.2.
B.2.2
Procedure
• Put samples in containers and note the weight.
• For hydration in oven, place the samples in the oven for the desired time. For hy­
dration at room atmosphere, leave them out.
• After the time has passed, weigh the samples and note the weight for the change in
mass to be found.
B.2.3
Results
Several experiments were performed. The specimens looked into is CaO and 13.7% skin
carbonated CaO. In Figure B.2 the weight change of CaO is shown as a function of time
in the oven at 105 °C. It clearly shows, that weight increases over time, when the CaO is
untreated beforehand. However, the specimen will only be heated for up to 1 h, meaning
that it will only gain around 0.5% weight during this time according to the figure. This
corresponds to 1.56% hydration, which can affect some results in other experiments, so
it has to be taken into account.
y = 0.4695x0.651
R² = 0.9993

The 13.7% skin carbonated sample has also been examined. The weight change over
time has not been looked into, but after 1 h in the oven at 105 °C, it only lost 0.1% in
weight. So heating the skin carbonated specimen at 105 °C for 1 h during experiments
must not be worried about.
When storing the specimens at room temperature with a relative humidity of around 50%
to check the hydration rate, the specimens were stored for 30 min. The results show, that

95
==PAGE:106==
pure CaO hydrates 0.604% while the skin carbonated CaO only hydrated up to 0.069%
which is practically nothing. This means, that when handling CaO in experiments, it must
avoid air exposure as much as possible, however it is to not be of any concerns. Skin
carbonated CaO is less vulnerable for air exposure, proving that treating the specimen
when e.g. sieving and crushing it will not affect the material.

Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials
==PAGE:107==
B.3
Degree of carbonation check
B.3.1
Purpose
The purpose with this experiment is to control the degree of carbonation of a sample. This
is done by checking the weight change of a sample after heating to 1050°C. This can be
done in two ways; By Thermogravimetric Analysis (TGA) or by heating the sample in a
special oven. The advantage of using the TGA is, that the weight change will be measured
throughout the heating process, so the change in weight can be observed. The downside
is, that only one specimen can be heated at a time. Also, the TGA was broken during
these experiments, meaning that heating the samples in the oven was the only way.
B.3.2
Procedure
• Weigh off a sample of the carbonated material desired to check for degree of car­
bonation. Note the sample weight.
• Place the sample in the oven at 1050°C for around 30 min assuring the whole spec­
imen has been heated up.
• Gently take the sample out of the oven while carefully placing it in a desiccator
assuring a low risk of atmosphere exposure.
• After cooling, weigh the sample again, and the degree of carbonation can be found.
Remember to take the ignition loss into account.
Shrinkage compensation of cement­based materials

clear all; clc;
 
data = xlsread('Data_temperature_setting_time') ;
 
%%
% MgO100 10% 1;3 , Ref 0.36 6;8, CaO 9% 11;13 , Sand 10% 0.36 16;18 , 
% Ref 0.30 21;23 , 19.4% Skin CaO 3% 26;28 , 12.4% Skin CaO 2.35% 31;33,
% 12.4% Skin CaO 6% 36;38 , 12.4% Skin CaO 3% < 40 µm 41;43
 
N_1 = 41;
N_2 = 43;
Time = data(3:end,N_1);
Temp = data(3:end,N_2);
 
% Til plottet bliver der printet coefficienter ud til linjerne. 
% Intercept er b, x1 er a, altså y = x1*x + intercept = a*x + b
 
%%%%%% Plot the entire data
% figure(2)
% plot(Time,Temp)
% ylim([28,32])
% xlim([min_time,max_time])
 
% Set min and max of time interval
min_time = 0;    
max_time = 12;     
 
%%%%%%% Plot interval
% figure(2)
% plot(Time,Temp)
% ylim([29,32])
% xlim([min_time,max_time])
 
%%%%%%% The analysis and plot
% Extract the subset to look at
[val,index_min] = min(abs(Time-min_time));
[val,i_max] = min(abs(Time-max_time));
 
[val,index_max] = max(Temp(index_min:i_max));
 
index_max = index_max+index_min;
 
data_fit = cat(2,Time(index_min:index_max+1,:),Temp(index_min:index_max+1,:))
 
num_clusters = 2; % Set 3 at CaO 9%
 
% k-means clustering
idx = kmeans(data_fit,num_clusters,'Distance','cityblock');    % cosine, sqeuclidean
 
% Seperate the data from the two classes
 
if N_1 == 12
    data_1 = data_fit(find(idx==3),:);
    data_2 = cat(1,data_fit(find(idx==1),:),data_fit(find(idx==2),:));
else

    data_1 = data_fit(find(idx==1),:);
    data_2 = data_fit(find(idx==2),:);

% Fit linear regression to the two parts
linear_model_1 = fitlm(data_1(:,1),data_1(:,2))
coefs_1 = linear_model_1.Coefficients.Estimate; % 2x1 [intercept; slope]
 
linear_model_2 = fitlm(data_2(:,1),data_2(:,2))
coefs_2 = linear_model_2.Coefficients.Estimate; % 2x1 [intercept; slope]
 
% Calculate intersection between the two lines
x = (coefs_1(1)-coefs_2(1))/(coefs_2(2)-coefs_1(2));
y = coefs_1(2)*x+coefs_1(1);
 
% Plot result
figure(1)
plot(Time,Temp,'LineStyle','-','Color','[0.6, .0, .0]')    % plot points,  % Color is 
DTU red
hline = refline(coefs_1(2),coefs_1(1));           % plot line 1
hline.Color = '[0.1843, .2431, .9176]';  % Color is DTU blue
hline = refline(coefs_2(2),coefs_2(1));           % plot line 2
hline.Color = '[0.1843, .2431, .9176]'; % Color is DTU blue
ylim([29.5,32]) 
xlim([min_time,max_time])
ylabel('Temperature [°C]')
xlabel('Time[h]')
 
strValues = strtrim(cellstr(num2str([x y],'(%.2f,%.2f)')));   % convert intersection 
point to a string
text(x,y,strValues,'VerticalAlignment','bottom');             % print intersection in 
plot
 
==PAGE:125==
 
 
 
 
Letbrændt pulveriseret kalk (0-0,2 mm) 
September 2019 
 
Letbrændt pulveriseret kalk. CaO 
 
(0-0,2 mm) 
 
Fremstilling 
Letbrændt pulveriseret kalk fremstilles ved brænding af knust kalksten med 
efterfølgende pulverisering.  
 
 
I kalkovnen udsættes kalkstenen for en stadig højere temperatur, og i selve 
brændzonen er temperaturen 1100-1200 °C. 
 
 
Ved brændingen uddrives kuldioxid (CO2) af kalkstenen (CaCO3), og der 
dannes brændt kalk (CaO): 
 
 
CaCO3 
+ varme → 
CaO 
+ CO2 
 
Kalksten + varme → 
brændt kalk + kuldioxid 
 
Anvendelse 
Letbrændt pulveriseret kalk finder anvendelse inden for mange segmenter. Det 
være sig bygge og anlæg, industrien, kraft/varmeværker, medicinalbranchen, 
levnedsmiddelbranchen med mere. Formålene er mange lige fra bindemiddel, 
neutralisering, rensning, pH regulering med mere.  
 
Analysedata 
Kalk er et naturprodukt, hvis sammensætning varierer inden for visse grænser. 
Samtlige analysedata skal derfor betragtes som retningsgivende. De kemiske 
og fysiske egenskaber beskrives af samme grund ved en middelværdi ( x ) og 
tilhørende standardafvigelse (s). Der er dog ingen øvre grænse for aktiv 
calciumoxid (CaOaktiv) 
 
 
Middelværdien plus/minus to gange standardafvigelsen ( x  ± 2 · s) angiver 
grænserne, inden for hvilke 95 % af vore analyseresultater vil falde.
